Real Estate
How Techies Are Transforming San Francisco; A generation of technology workers is bringing startups, venture capitalistsand high real-estate prices
Sarah Tilton
602 words
9 January 2014
14:33
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Twitter's headquarters. A high-rise apartment building where visitors sign-in on iPads. A jar of handcrafted applesauce for $14. Skyrocketing real-estate prices.

Nearly five decades after the Summer of Love transformed San Francisco into the epicenter of the hippie movement, a new generation is redefining this city's culture again. No longer content to live and work in the quiet suburbs of Palo Alto and Menlo Park 30 miles south, thousands of young tech workers are migrating to the city, seeking a more urban, multicultural lifestyle. They are bringing with them a stampede of tech companies and venture capitalists, and inevitably attracting some homegrown resentment for jacking up housing costs and gentrifying once gritty neighborhoods.

Last year, venture capitalist Greg Gretsch, managing director at Sigma West, which has $1.5 billion under management, moved his office from Menlo Park to San Francisco's Jackson Square neighborhood, a historic part of the city dating back to the 1850s that is now attracting the new digerati. He now walks to work from his Pacific Heights home and walks or bikes to visit startups in the South of Market, or Soma, district.

"Jackson Square is the new Sand Hill Road," says Mr. Gretsch, 47, referring to the road in Menlo Park that has long been at the center of technology funding. "I'm surprised by how infrequently I go down [to Silicon Valley] because I just don't need to."

The cost of living is high among the new tech class. The median sales price for homes in San Francisco jumped 16% in 2013, according to the San Francisco Association of Realtors. "It was a year of shocking prices," says Annie Williams, a real-estate agent with Hill & Co. in San Francisco. She ticks off the names of southern neighborhoods closest to Silicon Valley, such as the Mission and Noe Valley, that have been most affected by the tech boom. "It was shock after shock started by Zuckerberg." In 2012, it was widely reported that Facebook founder Mark Zuckerberg paid $9.9 million for a house in the city's Mission District. A Facebook spokesman declined to comment.

In 2013, an 11,000-square-foot house in Pacific Heights on San Francisco's Gold Coastwhere neighbors include Apple's Jonathan Ive and Oracle billionaire Larry Ellisonsold for $35 million. In 2011, the house sold for $29.5 million.

Not everyone likes the changes wrought by the technocrat class. Protesters attempting to highlight the issue of rising rents in the Mission District recently tried to stop the private buses that take tech workers to their jobs at companies including Google, Facebook and Apple. Musician Adam Theis, founder of the long-standing musical collective JazzMafia, hasn't joined the protests but is among those who have been forced to leave the Mission after his rent for a two-bedroom apartment soared to $3,000 a month from $1,400.

Mr. Theis, 39, had lived in the Mission since 1997. "I was really lucky to have had that house for 15 years; it allowed us to have a place where we could create," he says, adding that his apartment building was known as a gathering place for artists and musicians. Mr. Theis now lives in Oakland, but still has gigs in the Mission. "It's not that people are trying to clear-cut or drive people outthere's a balance missing of cultural consciousness," he says.

GOLDMAN SACHS

Asian Financing Group

Gets New Co-Chiefs

Goldman Sachs Group Inc. promoted Jonathan Penkin and Andrea Vella as co-heads of an Asian regional financing group, which handles bond and share sales, including initial public offerings, according to an internal memo.

The changes, which cover the Asian-Pacific region except Japan, came as Dan Dees, most recently co-head of investment banking for the region, was made co-head of Goldman's global technology, media and telecommunications banking unit, alongside Anthony Noto. Matthew Westerman, who was co-head, will become sole head of the investment-banking division in Asia Pacific excluding Japan. Mr. Penkin was head of equity capital markets for Asia excluding Japan before the promotion. Mr. Vella was head of credit capital markets in Asia excluding Japan and the investment-banking solutions teams in Asia Pacific.

-- Prudence Ho

---

HEDGE FUNDS

Bridgewater Returns

Trail Industry Average

Bridgewater Associates, the world's biggest hedge-fund firm, had a rare disappointing year in 2013, returning 5.3% in its flagship Pure Alpha fund and losing 3.9% in its widely held All Weather fund, according to people familiar with the matter.

Bridgewater, based in Westport, Conn., managed $150 billion as of June. The firm's returns trailed the overall industry average of 9.3%, according to data tracker HFR, while macro funds like Bridgewater's on average lost 0.3%.

Bridgewater's All Weather fund, which invests using an increasingly popular "risk-parity" strategy, was down about 8% as of June. Risk-parity funds aim to equally distribute risk among stocks, bonds and commodities. "We don't expect to make money every year," Bob Prince, co-chief investment officer of Bridgewater, told The Wall Street Journal this year.

-- Juliet Chung

---

REAL ESTATE

Developer Sunac Expects

19% Increase in Sales

High-end property developer Sunac China Holdings Ltd. expects sales to rise 19% this year, a sign of its confidence that demand from Chinese home buyers will remain strong.

China's housing sector has outperformed its slowing economy, with sharp rises in housing sales and prices in major cities driving the gains in real estate. Average housing prices in 100 Chinese cities rose 11.5% in December from the same month a year earlier, according to private data provider China Real Estate Index System. Over the same period, housing prices in Beijing and Shanghai rose 28% and 16% respectively.

Sunac Chairman Sun Hongbin said the company remains focused on building in China's major cities, where prices have been strongest. The firm expects sales to top 65 billion yuan ($10.7 billion) this year, from 54.7 billion yuan last year.

-- Esther Fung

---

J.P. MORGAN CHASE

IMF Official to Oversee

Asia Regulatory Strategy

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. has hired Anoop Singh, a top official at the International Monetary Fund, as the bank's head of regulatory strategy in Asia, a new role as the U.S. lender grapples with a probe into its hiring practices in the region.

Mr. Singh, currently the IMF's Asia Pacific head, will start next month in Hong Kong as head of regulatory strategy and policy for the region, according to a memo seen by The Wall Street Journal.

U.S. regulators are examining whether J.P. Morgan hired the children of Chinese officials in an effort to win business for its Asian investment-banking operations. Investigators are looking for violations of the U.S. Foreign Corrupt Practices Act, a 1977 law that bars U.S.-based or U.S.-listed companies from giving money or other items of value to foreign officials to win business.

-- Enda Curran

Business
Sunac China Sees Sales Up 19% This Year; High-End Property Developer Confident Demand Will Remain Strong
Esther Fung
612 words
8 January 2014
05:17
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SHANGHAIHigh-end property developer Sunac China Holdings said it expects its sales to rise 19% this year in a sign of its confidence that demand from Chinese home buyers will remain strong.

China's housing sector has outperformed its slowing economy, with sharp rises in housing sales and prices in major cities driving the gains in real estate. Average housing prices in 100 Chinese cities rose 11.5% in December from the same month a year earlier, according to private data provider China Real Estate Index System. Over the same period, housing prices in Beijing and Shanghai rose 28.3% and 15.6% respectively.

"I'm still confident of the business of selling high-end homes, as the buying power of Chinese consumers remains strong," said Sunac Chairman Sun Hongbin at a press briefing Wednesday. He said the company remains focused on building in China's major cities only, where prices have been strongest.

The company expects sales to top 65 billion yuan (about $10.7 billion) this year, up 19% from 54.7 billion yuan last year. The firm's housing sales in 2013 rose 54% from 2012. In the first half of 2013, the developer earned profit totaling 750 million yuan, up 43% from a year earlier. It didn't offer a full-year figure on Wednesday.

Sunac, in which Bain Capital LLC has a roughly 5% stake, was nicknamed by local media as a "land king" for buying land in major cities last year. In September it purchased a Beijing plot for what analysts estimated was an average cost of 73,100 yuan per square meter, which they said was one of the highest prices recorded in the nation's capital.

"Buying land in Beijing is very competitive. Every Chinese developer wants to build in Beijing," said Mr. Sun, shrugging off concerns that Sunac may have overpaid for the plot.

Some local governments in major Chinese cities had taken more measures late last year to rein in the housing prices. Beijing's municipal government said in October it will sharply increase the supply of land for the construction of 70,000 private housing units by 2014 that will be 30% lower than neighboring housing prices.

"Not every location is suitable for high-end homes, and not every developer can build high-end homes," Mr. Sun said, shrugging off the impact of low cost housing too.

Some analysts said they expect China's property market to slow down this year, and that pent-up demand for homes was more or less absorbed last year. They also say the property market in China is unbalanced, with the larger cities facing stubbornly strong housing prices while smaller cities known as tier-three and tier-four cities struggle with excessive supply.

Another wrinkle in the high-end housing market would be the expansion of property taxes. Chinese officials have said that they intend to speed up property tax legislation, which would help rein in speculation and tackle the issue of empty homes in the country if owners of multiple homes are taxed on holding property. Property taxes are currently implemented in Shanghai and Chongqing, and analysts widely expect more cities to follow suit.

"China's property market prospects differ across the country, and in some places, the differences are very sharp. How the property market pans out in different cities would also depend on how the local authorities handle their local debt situation," said Mr. Sun. China's cabinet last month stepped up regulation of its "shadow-banking" or informal lending system.

Europe
Norway's House Prices Set to Fall; First Decline Since 2008 Follows Boom that Has Left Many People Unable to Afford to Buy Property
Kjetil Malkenes Hovland
696 words
6 January 2014
06:29
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

OSLONorwegian property prices are set to fall for the first time since 2008 amid an increase in housing supply and as borrowers face stiffer requirements from lenders, according to Norway's real-estate broker association.

The association Monday forecast a decline of 1% to 3% in prices in 2014 and follows price falls that started taking place at the end of last year. It is the latest in a series of gloomy outlooks for the market.

Christian Vammersvold Dreyer, chief executive of the EEF, Norway's real-estate broker association, said the rise in Norway's property prices over the past few years has been too steep. A strong domestic oil and gas industry has fueled the economy, significantly improving Norwegians' living standards even as other parts of Europe suffer.

"In recent years, [the housing price] growth has been too strong compared with what we want," Mr. Dreyer said. "Prices are starting to approach a sustainable and reasonable level."

There are several factors behind the projected slowdown, including a recent uptick in building activity. In addition, banks now require borrowers to hold more capital to secure loans.

The average price growth slowed down to 4.9% on the year in 2013 from 7.7% in 2012, the association said. In December, the prices fell compared with the year-earlier period for the first time since 2009.

As Norway's economy has sizzled, unemployment has dropped to among the lowest rates in the world and households have been anxious to spend money. Following a 4% price fall amid the financial crisis in 2008, property prices rebounded to 3% growth in 2009, 8% growth in 2010 and 9% growth in 2011.

Housing prices have become prohibitively high for the likes of Ulrika gren, a 38-year-old single restaurant worker from Sweden who lives in a rented apartment in Oslo with her five-year-old son.

"I can't afford to buy in Norway," she said in an interview. "If you're single with a child, you can't borrow three times your salary."

Ms. gren said she pays NOK10,000 ($1,630) a month for a small two-bedroom flat, which is considered cheap by Norwegian standards. She is happy that prices were expected to fall this year.

"The prices are wild," Ms. gren said. "At the bank they've told me twice, 'find a man.'"

But those fortunate enough to own real estate have benefited.

Ian Mikkelborg, 44, said his house in Fredrikstad in southern Norway has doubled in value since he bought it in 2002, but had also added an extra floor. He isn't surprised by the slowdown in the housing market.

"I think it's a natural consequence," Mr. Mikkelborg said. "There's been an extraordinary growth. But nothing grows into heaven."

Norges Bank , Norway's central bank , has forecast a soft landing for the housing market. The EFF also expects the housing market to remain strong in the longer term, due to fundamental factors such as very low unemployment at around 3.5%, high immigration of foreign workers, solid wage growth and slowing construction activity from this year.

Those projections, however, appear to be modest compared with other analysts.

"We believe in a bigger drop," Nordea Markets senior analyst Katrine Godding Boye said, forecasting a 7% to 8% housing price fall in 2014. "We don't believe in the expected upswing in the forecasts from Norges Bank and the EFF."

Nordea Markets expects weaker construction in 2014 amid the slowing housing market, and expects lower growth in oil sector investments, contributing to slower economic growth. "We think there will be rate cuts this summer, Ms. Godding Boye said. "We'll see real weakness in key indicators this spring."

The EFF didn't forecast prices beyond 2014, but Mr. Dreyer acknowledged that slowing growth in Norway's dominant oil sector in the coming years may weigh on property prices. "If the Norwegian economy cools down, the housing market will also feel it," he said.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Opinion
A Good Time for Housing Reform; The FHA has missed its 2% minimum capital standard for five years.
573 words
6 January 2014
15:53
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The prospects for progress on Capitol Hill this year are few, but one possibility is reform of federal housing policy. With home prices rising and the economy growing, now is a good time to pare back the government support that has too often led to unsustainable housing booms and taxpayer busts.

Consider the Obama Administration's latest report to Congress on the Federal Housing Administration. The Department of Housing and Urban Development noted last month that the FHA has a negative $1.3 billion net worth, and that's after a $1.7 billion Treasury capital infusion in September to offset losses. FHA insures more than $1 trillion in loans and has now missed its federally mandated 2% minimum capital standard for five years.

This is hard to do amid a housing recovery, even for the government. FHA wasn't caught up in the subprime hurricane as Fannie Mae or Freddie Mac were in the pre-2007 boom years. Instead, FHA expanded its high-risk book of business right as everyone else was getting out.

As HUD Secretary Shaun Donovan euphemistically put it, "the substantial role FHA was forced to play" to keep housing credit flowing during the bust inflicted "considerable stress." Uh huh. Delinquency rates on FHA's 2007, 2008 and 2009 books stand at 26%, 26% and 19%. Taxpayers will now have to pay for those homeowners who were induced in part by FHA subsidies to buy more home than they could afford.

The Obama Administration's response to FHA's troubles has been to pile higher fees on new borrowers. Along with rising housing prices and the quiet shuttering of FHA's reverse-mortgage business, this has helped FHA's finances improve from awful to merely rotten, but it may not be enough to right the books soon.

It's hard to know how deep the FHA's red ink goes. The agency's fiscal health is an estimate based on 30-year forecasts of mortgage prepayment rates, unemployment rates, house prices and inflation rates that no private insurer would use. Do you know what unemployment will be in 2044?

Even the actuaries disagree on the scope of the problem. FHA's go-to firm, Integrated Financial Engineering Inc., thinks the agency's single-family home insurance program is worth negative $7.9 billion but could make money by the end of the fiscal year. Summit Consulting and Milliman Inc., more conservative outfits, think the right number is negative $10.6 billion and the business will remain in the red next year, adding that forecasts are "subject to significant variability."

Whatever the real number, the main goal should be not to repeat the mistake. The underlying problem is that the FHA's woes derive from a policy of public risk and private profit, which is what blew up Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.

In a better world, Congress would slowly work off the FHA's portfolio and get out of the housing subsidy business. Short of that, Texas Republican Jeb Hensarling has a proposal to spin off FHA from the Department of Housing and Urban Development, make it a stand-alone agency, focus its mission on low-income and first-time home buyers, and subject it to the same accounting rules that private lenders have to meet. How about starting there?

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

MANSION --- Private Properties
By Candace Taylor
1013 words
3 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J

English

Queen Latifah Lists in New Jersey

Queen Latifah, the musician, actress and talk-show host, has listed her New Jersey home for $2.399 million with Coldwell Banker Residential Brokerage, the firm said.

Over 7,500 square feet, the home has six bedrooms, five bathrooms and three half baths. It sits on roughly 9 acres in Colts Neck, N.J., in Monmouth County, according to the listing agent Robin Jackson. The home also has an exercise room and game room. On the grounds is a five-car garage and a heated, Olympic-size swimming pool.

Queen Latifah -- the stage name used by Dana Owens -- is originally from New Jersey, and the house was the site of many family gatherings hosted by the Oscar-nominated actress, Ms. Jackson said.

But since the debut of her daytime talk show, "The Queen Latifah Show," Ms. Owens is spending more of her time in California, Ms. Jackson said.

"Many incredible memories have been shared with those closest to me and my family in this home," Ms. Owens said in a statement. "I am confident that the new owners will love the home and the Colts Neck community as much as I do."

Ms. Owens bought the home for $1.685 million from a developer in 2001 when it was partially completed, Ms. Jackson said.

On display on the ground floor of the home is a Yamaha piano emblazoned with a New York City skyline. The piano was played by Alicia Keys in the Jay Z music video for "Empire State of Mind." Ms. Owens bought the piano at a charity auction. But "I don't think she's going to sell the piano," Ms. Jackson said.

Two $20 Million-Plus

House Sales Recorded

In Orange County

It was a big year for real estate in California's affluent Orange County. First, a $22.25 million sale set a record for the town of Dana Point. Then a mansion in Laguna Beach sold for $33 million, in what agents say was the county's priciest home sale of 2013.

The Laguna Beach home is in the private community of Irvine Cove. According to public records, the waterfront home sold in October for $33 million. Rob Giem of HOM Sotheby's International Realty had listed the 7,200-square-foot, five-bedroom, nine-bathroom home a little less than year ago for $41.5 million, and the price was later reduced to $38 million.

The seller, Kenneth J. Sullivan, couldn't be reached to comment. The buyer is a New York City-based entity called Iconicviews Co. LLC.

The Dana Point home, also on the waterfront, is in a gated community called the Strand at Headlands. The house was sold in June after being listed by HOM's John Stanaland for nearly $25 million. According to Mr. Stanaland, the property is the highest priced sale ever recorded in Dana Point, where prices have historically been lower than in Laguna Beach or Newport Beach.

According to public records, the home was sold by Geoffrey and Ashley Rosenhain to Jon and Tara Darbyshire. Mr. Rosenhain, the founder of Laguna Beach-based Jado Investments, declined to comment.

Coming This Spring: A New York City Townhouse for Over $20 Million

Once part of New York City's William Gottlieb estate, a brick townhouse on Horatio Street in the West Village will hit the market this spring for more than $20 million, according to the owners.

Mr. Gottlieb started buying downtown Manhattan property in the 1950s, and by the time of his death in 1999, he owned more than 100 properties estimated to be worth as much as $1 billion. After his death, a feud among his heirs made headlines, while retail and residential tenants complained that the buildings had fallen into disrepair. A spokesperson for the Gottlieb estate said the family maintained its buildings well, as evidenced by the high prices they have sold for.

This Horatio Street property, built in the 1880s, was sold by the estate in 2011. A joint venture of Greystone Property Development and developer Mark Blau bought it in 2012 for about $10 million, said Greystone's Jeffrey Simpson.

The current owners are converting the 10-unit apartment building into a 7,000-square-foot, five bedroom, single-family mansion with an elevator, seven bathrooms and three half bathrooms, Mr. Simpson said. Greystone is restoring the facade of the townhouse, including rebuilding a stoop that was removed. When completed, the house will also have a roof deck, a wine cellar and a media room. At the back, folding glass walls will lead out to a 1,000-square-foot garden.

The property is being marketed by Fredrik Eklund and John Gomes of Douglas Elliman Real Estate. Mr. Gomes said the property will likely be listed in late February or early March, "when the market wakes up," for a price over $20 million, likely in the "mid to low 20s."

Actor Leonardo DiCaprio has sold his beachfront Malibu home for $17.35 million, Curbed.com reported. The property, on Malibu Colony Road, initially hit the market in 2012 asking $23 million. It was listed by Kathryn Bentzen of Arete Estates. The actor purchased the home for $6 million in 2002 and completed a major renovation, Ms. Bentzen said, adding that it rented for the past two summers for $175,000 a month.

Singer Sheryl Crow has been trying to sell her Tennessee equestrian estate for more than three years, and the property has received a major price cut, according to Zillow Blog. The Grammy Award-winner first listed Cross Creek Farms, located in College Grove, in 2010 for $7.5 million. Now the 51-acre property has been relisted at $3.85 million with Jay Barron of Harpeth Company. In addition to a six-bedroom house, Mr. Barron said, the property has a 14-stall horse barn -- complete with a Western-style saloon -- as well as an indoor riding arena and a saltwater infinity pool.

MANSION --- The Market: Big Sky, Little Ranch --- Bozeman, Mont., is becoming known as the place to buy a ranchette, a smaller, more-connected alternative to living on one of the state's famous sprawling ranches
By Caroline Porter
948 words
3 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M3
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Life on Montana's sprawling ranches and open ranges is being trimmed down in the mountain town of Bozeman, where some homeowners are choosing another lifestyle: the ranchette.

One part city home and one part ranch, the ranchette is a compromise for homeowners looking for a taste of the Montana lifestyle with the conveniences of Bozeman's downtown district. Such properties are typically situated within a 10-to-15-minute drive to Main Street, and come with land from 10 to 50 acres, rather than in the thousands. Still, the properties tend to boast mountain views and quick access to sports and outdoor activities.

"It's not a giant, unmanageable piece of land," said Ben Heller, who purchased 28 acres with a main house and a guesthouse for $1.6 million with his wife, Colby Hall, in 2012. Their second home is surrounded by farm land, mountains and two creeks.

"I'm not a farmer, nor do I want to be," he added. "But it's enough land to have the feeling of being somewhat secluded in nature."

With one child and another on the way, the 40-year-old Mr. Heller, who works for an investment firm, said he wanted a place for his family to hike and to fish when they escape the heat of Austin, Texas, summers.

Bozeman, known as a four-season town with a population of about 39,000 and mountains in every direction, also hosts Montana State University, a symphony orchestra and an airport within about a 20-minute drive, with direct flights to New York City, Chicago and Los Angeles.

According to the local Realtors' association, the number of property sales continue to rise. For the year 2010,19 properties sold ranging in price from $190,000 to about $5 million. From December 2011 to December 2012, the number jumped to 30 sold properties, with prices from $260,000 to $2.1 million. From December 2012 to December 2013, 35 properties have been sold, ranging in price from about $300,000 to $2.2 million.

For Mike Hunt and his wife, Norma, who are based in El Paso, Texas, it took a while to find the right fit. After visiting friends in the region for nearly a decade for fly fishing, Mr. Hunt, an executive in a family-held business, began looking for a second home in southwestern Montana, but found that it didn't accommodate both of their needs.

"My wife said, 'I don't want to live in a resort. I want to live in a normal, regular community.' So that started us looking in this area in September 2012."

The Hunts purchased their Bozeman home in December 2012. It is located just south of the city -- a 10-minute drive into the downtown. The listing price for the house and accompanying 20 acres was $2.65 million.

They have renovated the property to include an outdoor living space with a fireplace and back patio, and turned a miniature soccer field into a landing pad for Mr. Hunt's helicopter. "Our favorite thing is the location, the setting and views," said 66-year-old Mr. Hunt.

Montana landowners are often passionate about preserving the land and its views, said Penelope Pierce, the executive director of the Gallatin Valley Land Trust, which works with landowners to prevent overdevelopment and conserve the region's natural habitat and its agriculture.

"We are seeing a lot of bigger properties getting carved into 20-acre ranchettes," said Ms. Pierce.

She points to cluster developments, where homeowners share hundreds of acres and live on smaller plots.

"Everything else is shared open space," she said of the cluster communities. "Those can be good models."

One such development, known as Eagle Rock Reserve, is home to Larry Brutger, who recently purchased a five-bedroom house on about 20 acres with his wife and two sons.

While Mr. Brutger declined to share his closing price, real-estate experts say the homes typically range in price from $1 million to $2.5 million.

"We went 45, 50 miles outside of town to look at 1,000-acre ranches. We loved them but the thing is, you're 45 minutes from nowhere," said the 51- year-old real-estate developer from St. Cloud, Minn.

"Eagle Rock is just perfect. It totally fit the bill," he added. "We have deer and elk going through the backyard with 360-degree views of the mountains."

World
U.K. House Prices at Heights Not Seen Since 2008; Latest Increase Adds to Fears That Prices Are Rising Too Fast
Ilona Billington
565 words
3 January 2014
08:42
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

U.K. house prices rose to their highest level in more than five years in December, while mortgage activity surged in November, data showed Friday, adding to concerns that a lack of supply is helping fuel rapid price increases.

The jump was again heavily driven by London, where house prices were nearly twice as high as the rest of the country. The divergence has sparked debate at the highest levels of government over whether there is an inflating bubble, and how it could be contained.

U.K. lender Nationwide said Friday that the average U.K. house price was 175,826 (about $288,000) in December, 8.4% higher than a year earlier, marking the fastest year-on-year rise since June 2010.

In London, average house prices jumped 15% in the fourth quarter to 345,186, from the same period in 2012, according to Nationwide's regional breakdown, which is done quarterly. This was biggest growth in nearly four years.

However, house prices in some parts of Londonsuch as the borough of Hammersmith and Fulhamjumped as much as 25% in the period, suggesting significant distortions in the hot market.

Demand from foreign investors is a big driver of the capital's soaring prices. Real estate in the choicest neighborhoods is viewed as a safe investment.

In London, stories of fierce demand amid soaring prices are common conversation. When 17 apartments in a new development in the trendy borough of Hackney went on sale in November, several people camped out on the sidewalk on a cold, rainy night and around 100 more were lined up by the time doors opened at 1 p.m, according to Lucy Phipps, sales director at estate agent Bridge. "It was crazy," she said.

While London prices surged, Nationwide said that house prices in the fourth quarter rose across all 13 U.K. regions for a second straight quarter.

This development was key to the Bank of England's recent announcement that it would remove mortgages from the Funding for Lending Scheme, a program aimed at encouraging banks to lend.

Data from the central bank on Friday showed that November mortgage approvals totaled 70,758the highest in five years, which implies the trend will continue in the new year.

"Affordability is being supported by the ultralow level of interest rates," said Robert Gardner, Nationwide's chief economist. "However, the risk is that if demand continues to run ahead of supply in the quarters ahead, affordability may become stretched."

There are signs, though, that the construction of new homes is rising, albeit from a low base and still some way off the 250,000 needed each year to keep pace with the country's growing population.

Data firm Markit on Friday said its construction purchasing managers index remained strong in December at 62.1, just down from November's 62.6, which was more than a six-year high.

"Historical comparisons with official data suggest that the PMI Housing Activity Index is signaling a rise of 40,000 in the number of homes being built in the fourth quarter, up sharply from the postcrisis low of just over 28,000 seen at the end of last year," said Chris Williamson, chief economist at Markit.

The Year Ahead: U.S.: Quick Takes
1367 words
2 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A7
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

HEALTH CARE

Law's Success Tied to Getting

Healthy People to Sign Up

The key benefits of the Affordable Care Act -- President Barack Obama's signature health overhaul -- will kick in Jan. 1, despite years of legal challenges and a glitch-plagued rollout.

Beginning New Year's Day, private health plans for individuals and small businesses will cover a broader range of benefits. Insurers will be barred from denying coverage to the sick. New federal subsidies will slash prices for many lower-income customers.

Many thousands also will gain coverage through the expansion of state-run Medicaid programs. States that chose not to expand the programs for low-income people will face pressure from hospitals to reverse course, which could further broaden coverage in 2014.

The law's ultimate success, however, depends on getting enough people, both sick and healthy, to sign up, a goal stymied by the disastrous October launch of the online marketplaces. Early technical stumbles for HealthCare.gov, the federal enrollment website serving 36 states, left far fewer people registered to get coverage on Jan. 1 than projected. Early enrollment data suggest that insurers will get more older -- and likely costlier -- customers than expected.

January also will test insurance companies' readiness. Insurers are still fixing flawed enrollment data and collecting payment from people signing up as late as New Year's Eve for coverage meant to start Jan. 1. Open enrollment will continue through March 31.

-- Christopher Weaver

MANUFACTURING

Industry Set to Grow Bolder

As Confidence Improves

U.S. manufacturers have several trends in their favor: Wages are about flat, while those in China are soaring. U.S. energy costs have fallen. And many U.S. companies want to stop relying so heavily on foreign plants, where quality and delivery times are hard to control.

Yet manufacturers generally have been cautious with investments amid worry over budget gridlock in Washington and a sluggish global economy. But they are likely to grow a bit bolder this year.

IHS Global Insight, an economic-research firm, expects that 2014 spending on equipment by manufacturing companies in the U.S. will rise about 7% from 2013 to $211 billion. Such spending grew 3.5% in 2013, IHS estimates.

"I think business people are pretty confident longer term," said Daniel Meckstroth, chief economist at the Manufacturers Alliance for Productivity and Innovation, a research organization in Arlington, Va. The two-year budget deal recently reached in Congress soothed nerves, and the U.S. economy appears to be gaining strength.

Some major investments are being made, particularly in the South.

Even so, the U.S. still relies heavily on products made overseas. The deficit on manufacturing trade totaled $568 billion in the first 10 months of 2013, narrowed only slightly from $574 billion a year earlier, Mr. Meckstroth calculates. U.S. manufacturers also face big challenges, including skill shortages, deteriorating highways and bridges, and higher taxes than most major foreign rivals.

-- James R. Hagerty and Cameron McWhirter

HOUSING

Recovery's Strength Depends

On First-Time Home Buyers

One group holds substantial influence over whether the housing recovery will continue or stall in 2014: first-time home buyers.

So far, the housing recovery has relied on investors and other well-heeled buyers who can pay in cash or qualify for mortgages under the more stringent standards of recent years. That was enough to push home prices to heady gains in 2013. But in recent months, sales volumes have slowed, especially for newly built homes.

Housing observers say the market can't return to its typical output until first-time buyers, who historically account for 30% to 40% of home sales, come back in force. First timers -- hampered in recent years by tepid job growth, greater competition, low supplies of homes for sale and tighter credit standards -- now make up about 20% of the new-home and 30% of the existing-home market. Tight lending standards could ease slightly in 2014, but higher prices and rising interest rates are making homes less affordable.

Key to watch in 2014: Will wage and job growth accelerate to boost organic demand and replace waning interest from investors? And will more inventory be added to the market to tamp down runaway price increases?

-- Kris Hudson

EDUCATION

Universities Face Squeeze

From Startups, Regulators

Look for pressure on traditional bricks-and-mortar universities to intensify in 2014 as they get squeezed between aggressive Silicon Valley startups offering cheaper, faster paths to credentials, and regulators aiming to stem a growing student debt crisis.

A demographic dip in graduating high-school seniors combined with the tech-driven unbundling of higher education's monopoly on degrees will moderate tuition increases and even prompt the decline of revenue growth for about one in four schools in 2014, according to Moody's Investors Service. With state subsidies for public universities stagnant, less-selective schools will continue to cut costs. The Ivy League and its competitive ilk will remain strong.

From Washington, renewal of the Higher Education Act will spark debate around accreditation. This spring, that fight will be accompanied by a raft of new bills from Democrats aimed at protecting student borrowers and timed to echo President Barack Obama's pledge to address the country's growing wealth gap. Then this summer, the first draft of Mr. Obama's college-rating system will arrive amid protests of the university establishment calling it unfair.

-- Douglas Belkin

TECHNOLOGY

Keep an Eye on Bitcoin,

Cloud Services and Video

Three growth areas in digital technology are worth watching in 2014: bitcoin, the cloud and video.

Bitcoin price volatility has heightened awareness of the virtual currency among retailers and investors -- and regulators. A few retailers, such as Overstock.com Inc., said they plan to begin accepting bitcoin. But legal concerns, as well as apprehension among financial institutions and governments, may keep bitcoin from the mainstream.

Cloud services are sure to grow, as firms and consumers lean on Web services that allow them to store data -- whether music or financial statements -- on faraway servers. The boom in smartphones and tablets, risky places to keep sensitive information, will accelerate the trend. Companies like Intel and Oracle that prospered by selling computer-related products are scrambling to build cloud businesses as older revenue sources shrink. Researchers at IDC say spending on cloud services and related technology will surge by 24% in 2014, topping $100 billion.

Web outlets like Netflix, YouTube and Amazon have been loosening networks' grip on Americans' TV habits for a while. But "cord cutting" was held back by people who wanted TV on a TV, not a computer. Increasingly, new devices make it easier for people to watch Web video on TVs. While people relying on Web video still need Internet access -- provided by cable operators -- big TV networks lose revenue as fewer people subscribe to pay-TV.

-- Andrew R. Johnson, Don Clark and Martin Peers

SURVEILLANCE

Fights Over NSA Programs

Are Likely to Escalate

Battles in Congress, courts and public opinion over the future of National Security Agency surveillance are likely to increase in 2014.

President Barack Obama has promised a January speech on proposals issued in mid-December by his review panel. A second panel, the Privacy and Civil Liberties Oversight Board, plans two reports in late January and early February -- one on the NSA's phone-data-surveillance program and another on the agency's use of U.S. infrastructure for foreign eavesdropping.

On Capitol Hill, Congress will debate competing measures. One bill by leaders of the House and Senate Judiciary committees would overhaul the NSA's phone program. Another backed by leaders of the intelligence committees would keep the program intact but add additional transparency and reporting requirements. Mr. Obama's position could tip the scales. The law undergirding the phone program expires in 18 months, which could force action.

Conflicting court rulings will head toward appeals. A federal judge in Washington ruled in December that the phone program is probably unconstitutional, but a judge in New York determined opposite.

Meanwhile, former NSA contractor Edward Snowden, whose leaks instigated the changes, is living under temporary asylum in Russia.

-- Siobhan Gorman

Singapore Home Prices Slip, Ending a Six-Quarter Growth Streak; Analysts Foresee Further Drops Ahead; Prices Are up 61% Since Mid-2009
Chun Han Wong
630 words
2 January 2014
08:58
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SINGAPOREPrivate-home prices in Singapore fell in the fourth quarter from the third, snapping six straight quarters of growth as government interventions crimped real-estate investment.

Property analysts say the declinethe first in nearly two yearscould portend further price falls this year, when large supplies of new homes are expected to reach the market amid a rise in interest rates.

The private-residential property price index fell 0.8% in the October-December period to 214.5 points, according to preliminary estimates issued Thursday by the Urban Redevelopment Authority. Prices last fell in the first quarter of 2012, before rising by 5% in the 18 months up to Septemberincluding a 0.4% increase in the third quarter last year and a 1% rise in the second quarter.

"Although another wave of activity is expected in light of more offerings in the pipeline, the various government measures have effectively curtailed demand from all groups of home buyers," said Chia Siew Chuin, director of research and advisory at property consultancy Colliers International. "Would-be buyers will also be concerned about the increase in interest rates."

The fourth-quarter price decline marked the private-housing market's worst showing since the second quarter of 2009the market's most recent cyclical troughwhen prices fell 4.7% from the quarter before. Despite the latest drop, private-home prices are up 61% since the end of June 2009.

Singapore has repeatedly tried to temper exuberance in its property market in recent years, imposing a series of cooling measures since September 2009 to combat upward price pressures from low interest rates and abundant capital inflows.

Regulators imposed curbs most recently in June, tightening property-loan rules to discourage imprudent borrowing ahead of a looming rise in interest rates. These followed January measures that mainly targeted foreign investors and residents who already owned homesstamp duties and down-payment requirements were raised while borrowing caps were tightened.

Such interventions helped tame private-housing sales this year. According to URA data, private developers in Singapore sold 14,951 new residences between January and November 2013nearly 30% fewer than in the year-earlier period.

Price growth has decelerated as a result, as developers adopt more competitive pricing strategies, analysts say. Private-home prices increased by 1.2% in 2013, after a 2.8% rise in 2012.

Sales may slow further this year thanks to potentially "stronger headwinds in the second half of 2014, given fewer attractive sites are ready for launch," said Christine Li, head of research at property consultancy OrangeTee. She expects private-home prices to fall between 2% to 5% over the next 12 months.

The fourth-quarter data suggest weaker demand for high-end housing and suburban homes. Prices for residences without land, such as apartments, in the core central region fell 2.2%, following a 0.3% decline in the third quarter. Prices in the rest of the central region rose 0.8%, reversing a 0.9% decline the quarter before..

Outside of the central region, where demand had been strongest in the past year, prices declined 0.6%the first drop since the second quarter of 2009, and following a 2.2% rise in the third quarter.

The latest URA data also mirror estimates from the Housing and Development Board, which showed prices of resold public housing declining 1.3% in the October-December period from the previous three months, extending a 0.9% slide in the third quarter.

Estimates are based on transaction prices compiled in the first 10 weeks of the quarter. Final figures will be issued in four weeks.

Australia House-Price Jump Rekindles Bubble Fears
James Glynn

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SYDNEYHome prices in Australia's main cities surged in 2013, deepening concern a possible housing bubble may be forming that could potentially derail a fragile economic recovery.

The country's property market ran hot again last year with prices nationally rising at the fastest pace in more than three years, fueled by record-low interest rates and growing demand for investment housing.

The sharp rises throughout past year have created some of the most expensive property in the world, fanning concern the market may be overheating and that a sudden crash could make jittery consumers even more nervous about spending.

The central bank has cut rates eight times in the past two years to try to get other sectors such as retail and housing to pick up and to compensate for a fading mining boom that is expected to dent the economy in coming years.

The RP Data-Rismark house-price report for December showed home values rose by 9.8% during the 2013 calendar year. Sydney, the country's largest property market, posted a 14.5% gain over the same period, while property prices in Melbourne and Perth also made strong advances. In December, home values nationally rose by 1.4% from November.

The housing figures came on the same day data showed manufacturing activity contracted in the last month of 2013. It was the second contraction in a row that erased expansions in September and October.

"This latest snapshot is yet another reminder of the urgency for Australia to put itself on a more balanced and diversified growth path," said Innes Willox, chief executive of AIG Group, which did the survey.

The disparity between surging house prices and weaker manufacturing highlights the key challenge facing Australia's economic-policy makers, who must find new sources of growth as the decadelong mining boom cools.

The central bank lowered its benchmark interest rate to 2.5% in August to help cushion the economy from a mining slowdown that is expected to push unemployment higher.

The resource-rich economy has already weakened after growing for 22 years straight. It expanded by 0.6% in the third quarter from the second, and by 2.3% from a year earlier, much slower than quarterly growth rates of as high as 4% in 2012.

Areas such as manufacturing, retail and tourism have been slow to respond to the lure of cheap credit, while investment in the mining sector slowed sharply last year. Consumer spending is showing only tentative signs of recovering.

With rates so low, the central bank has resorted to trying to jawbone down the Australian dollar, which has traded near historic highs for much of the past three years but has fallen below parity with the U.S. dollar in recent months. A high Aussie dollar makes life harder for exporters, particularly manufacturers, who lose out when repatriating overseas earnings.

Michael Workman, a senior economist at the Commonwealth Bank of Australia, said he expected house prices to continue to rise this year, making further rate cuts less likely.

"From a financial stability perspective, house price appreciation is something that the RBA will continue to watch closely in 2014," Mr. Workman said.

Paul Braddick, head of property research at Australia and New Zealand Banking Group, based in Melbourne, said it was too soon to be talking about a house-price bubble, saying fundamental economic factors were driving prices.

"Gains are largely explained by improved affordability, the release of pent-up sales and some catch up following earlier price falls," Mr. Braddick said.

SHANGHAIChina's housing market showed a modest pickup in December from November, new data show, as routine year-end difficulties in securing mortgages and a slower pace of new launches capped price growth.

Still, the release on Tuesday by data provider China Real Estate Index System showed prices grew 11.5% in December compared with one year ago, in the highest increase since August 2011. The result caps a year of increases that shows the challenge high housing prices presents to Beijing's leaders.

The survey tracks new-home sales in 100 Chinese cities. The average price in December rose 0.7% compared with November, the survey said. That is almost unchanged from 0.68% between November and October, which was the slowest on-month gain this year.

"The average month-on-month growth in housing prices was almost flat compared with November's and fewer cities recorded growth rates of more than 1%," said CREIS. "This shows that housing prices are stabilizing."

"With expectations that housing supply would increase next year, many consumers chose to remain on the sidelines to wait for new projects," said CREIS, referring to 2014.

While November and December are typically slower periods for home sales, some observers have warned that housing sales in China may cool in 2014 because many first-time home-buyers have now entered the market.

According to the survey, fewer cities showed a pickup, with 68 cities registering higher month-on-month prices compared with 69 in November and 75 in October. Prices in the remaining cities fell. Prices in Xiangtan grew the fastest, by 3.49%, followed by Nanjing, where they grew 2.62%. In Beijing, prices rose 0.42%, up from November's 0.18% gain.

Policy makers are trying to avoid the risk of a sharper correction down the line and have implemented cooling measures to restrain home prices that vary in each city. Home prices in major cities, in particular, have risen sharply in 2013, driven partly by strong demand and the lack of new residential projects in downtown areas.

China will continue its property curbs in 2014 and increase land and housing supplies in cities where property prices are rising, the official Xinhua news agency cited Housing Minister Jiang Weixin as saying last week.

Policy makers' focus in 2014 is likely to be on ramping up the supply of cheaper homes in China's major cities, and the expansion of the property tax to more cities, which are more sustainable ways of reining in the housing market, analysts said.

The Chinese government launched a property-tightening campaign in 2010 to rein in frothy housing prices, but its results so far have been limited. It raised mortgage and down-payment rates for second-home buyers and imposed curbs on multiple home purchases, but they have been arbitrarily enforced by local governments, economists say. Local governments, which get a large chunk of their revenue from land sales, have an interest in keeping land and property prices high, analysts say.

Both the 10-city and 20-city indexes increased 0.2% in October from September on an unadjusted basis, slower than the 0.7% increase in the previous month.

The seasonally adjusted gain for both composites was 1.0% in October from September, in line with the gains seen in the previous month.

"The key economic question facing housing is the Fed's future course to scale back quantitative easing and how this will affect mortgage rates. Other housing data paint a mixed picture suggesting that we may be close to the peak gains in prices," he said.

Each one percentage-point increase in mortgage rates makes homes about 10% more expensive for buyers by increasing the monthly mortgage payments. Rates, however, remain near historical lows.

Nicole Hong

English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The dollar rose against major currencies on Tuesday, capping its biggest annual gain in five years.

The Wall Street Journal Dollar Index, which tracks the dollar's value against a basket of widely traded currencies, ended 2013 at 73.828, up 4.8% this year. This marks the index's biggest yearly rise since its 8% gain in 2008.

Among major currencies, the worst performer against the dollar was the yen, which has declined 21.4% this year as a consequence of the Japanese government's efforts to stimulate the economy. The yen depreciated to Y105.28 per dollar from Y86.74 per dollar at the start of the year.

Australia's currency also had a weak year amid slowing Chinese demand and falling prices for Australia's commodities exports. The Australian dollar was at $0.8916, down 14.2% against the U.S. dollar this year.

Also driving the greenback's gains was the expectation that the U.S. economy would heal enough for the Federal Reserve to start winding down its post-crisis stimulus program. The central bank announced on Dec. 18 that it would begin reducing, or "tapering," its stimulus in January. Central bank stimulus tends to weigh on the dollar's value.

The Fed is expected to keep gradually cutting down the stimulus program next year.

"We expect dollar strength to continue in the first half of 2014 as the Fed goes through with tapering," said Greg Anderson, global head of foreign exchange strategy at BMO Capital Markets.

However, the dollar did fall against European currencies. The best performer of 2013 among major currencies was the euro, which logged a 4.1% year-to-date gain against the dollar as the euro zone no longer teetered on the brink of crisis. This is the first time that Europe's common currency has led the pack since its inception 14 years ago. The euro closed out the year at $1.3742.

The Swiss franc came in second place with a 2.5% gain against the dollar this year, trading at CHF0.8926.

The dollar traded at 104.97 on a quiet day, down slightly from a high of 105.40 reached overnight. It also slipped against other major currencies as U.S. Treasury yields fell a from two-year high above 3%, diminishing the allure of the U.S. currency.

Still, U.S. economic data in recent weeks have reassured markets of a 2014 recovery for the world's biggest economy. The latest number of pending home sales, which rose for the first time in six months, point to stability in the housing market.

The euro slipped to $1.3792 on Tuesday, from as high as $1.3818 Monday, as banks continue to repatriate funds ahead of the European Central Bank's asset-quality review, an audit of the financial system. With banks continuing to sell overseas assets and bring back capital, the European currency has maintained gains in recent months, foreign-exchange strategists say, although it is now seen as vulnerable.

The Chinese yuan, up 2.8% for the year, hit a record on Tuesday at 6.05 against the U.S. dollar.

Most Asian currencies have lost ground against the dollar this year. The Indonesia rupiah is down 21%, the Indian rupee has fallen 12%, the Philippines peso has lost 8% of its value, and the Malaysian ringgit and Thai baht are both down almost 7%.

Strategists say the slight appreciation over the last two weeks in emerging Asian currencies against the U.S. dollar has been driven by low liquidity in holiday-thinned markets.

The South Korean won ended the year up 0.76%, having weathered the U.S. monetary policy shocks due to the country's strong trade balance. Analysts expect the won to maintain its strength into next year.

Taiwan, South Korea, the Philippines and Thailand foreign exchange markets were closed on Tuesday.

U.S. News: Home Prices Back at Peaks in Some Areas --- Recovery Remains Uneven as Cities Spared in Bust Soar, but Many Others Struggle; 'We Had No Bubble Here'
By Nick Timiraos
1250 words
31 December 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
6
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Home prices have zipped back into record territory in a handful of American cities, a milestone that comes seven years after the housing bust ravaged the market and the broader economy.

Values are up more than 13% from their 2007 high in Oklahoma City and by more than 6% in the Denver metro area. Prices are back to all-time highs in 10 of the nation's 50 largest metropolitan areas, according to a Wall Street Journal analysis of price data from Zillow, an online real-estate information service. Prices are within 5% of their previous peak in San Jose, Calif.; Nashville, Tenn.; and Dallas.

Prices nationally remain below the highs of the past decade, and many of the cities that have seen the biggest gains largely escaped a boom and bust.

Home prices in some parts of the country that did experience a bust have benefited from low supplies of homes for sale and historically low interest rates that have boosted prices-- and sparked concerns that prices could again be overvalued.

The figures aren't adjusted for inflation, but experts say they underscore the uneven nature of the U.S. housing recovery.

"The main story in a lot of these places is that they didn't have much of a housing recession. It's much easier to be back at peak levels when you didn't have a big boom and bust," said Stan Humphries, chief economist at Zillow.

But in those areas that did experience a downturn, he added, "I'm surprised that we are back to peak levels so quickly."

Brian Long of Colorado was surprised, too. At the top of the housing bubble in 2006, Mr. Long paid $667,500 for a five-bedroom home in Lafayette, Colo., which is about 30 kilometers northwest of Denver. Prices then fell 12% through 2011. This summer, he sold the home for $710,000.

"The opportunity came up, so we ran with it," said the 49-year-old Mr. Long, who runs a company that collects and sells market research.

The 10 metro areas enjoying a full-scale rebound are based on figures for the entire region. The Wall Street Journal also analyzed Zillow price data individually in more than 4,400 cities and towns in the country's largest metro areas. Nearly 10% of municipalities have seen prices reach new highs in 2013 when compared with their previous peak, and prices are within 5% of their previous highs in 300 more.

These cities are largely exceptions, and prices in many parts of the U.S. are still well below their peak. In some 1,500 cities, values are still at least 25% lower than their previous highs. Nationally, values fell 23.8% between 2007 and 2011 before rebounding 9.9% after hitting bottom in late 2011; they are now 16.3% below the high of the last decade, according to Zillow.

The Zillow data also reveal the extreme variation-- even within a particular metropolitan area-- of the housing boom, bust and recovery. Prices are up 40% from their prior highs in Palo Alto, Calif., which is just 80 kilometers from San Pablo, a working-class suburb north of Oakland. Values there are still 54% below their peak.

While 38% of all Bay Area ZIP Codes are back above their prior peaks, prices in another 18% are more than 25% below their previous highs, according to the Zillow data.

To find some of the nation's steadiest housing markets, head to the Great Plains and Texas, which have enjoyed strong job and population growth tied to the oil, mineral and gas industries.

"We had no bubble here, so there was really no reason for prices to drop," said Keith Taggart, a real-estate agent in Mustang, Okla., and president of the Oklahoma City Metro Association of Realtors. Nearly 2,000 people relocate to the area every month, creating demand for homes, he said. At the same time, there's plenty of land to develop, so prices don't rise in "any outrageous way," Mr. Taggart said.

In August, Gwen Thompson and her husband sold for $92,000 the three-bedroom home in Mustang that they paid $65,000 for in 1995.

"People keep moving here, and that really helped hold value," said Ms. Thompson, a 45-year-old executive assistant at a local bank. She and her husband broke ground last month on a $245,000 home that is being built one town over.

Nearly every ZIP Code in Oklahoma City's metro area is at a record high or within 5% of the previous high, according to the Journal analysis. Values in more than 60% of ZIP Codes in five other metro areas-- Tulsa, Okla.; Buffalo, N.Y.; Rochester, N.Y.; Austin and Denver-- have reached new highs.

The Zillow data compared prices in October with the previous decade's peak price, which for most cities was reached between 2005 and 2007. The company uses a proprietary model to estimate values, considering recent sales and appraisals to determine the median value of all homes for a given area.

Some well-off communities in coastal California, Boston and Washington, D.C., which saw modest price declines during the bust, are rebounding quickly and reaching new highs because of supply shortages and better-than-average job growth. Prices in Palo Alto, for example, have jumped nearly 19% in the year ending in October and stood nearly 40% above their 2007 peak, one of the largest gains in the survey.

"What you're looking at, for the most part, are high-end neighborhoods that never saw the same degree of inflation in the first place," said Christopher Thornberg, a housing economist with Beacon Economics in Los Angeles.

Some economists worry that home buyers along the coasts could again be looking at homes as investments rather than as places to live.

To the extent that gains aren't supported by rising rents or incomes, "you start to go, 'Geez, did people get a little too excited?' " said Thomas Lawler, an independent housing economist in Leesburg, Va.

It is unclear how much longer buyers will continue to bid prices higher. Buyers often tell Mia Simon, a real-estate agent with Redfin in Silicon Valley, that they are going to sit out the market. "They say, 'This is crazy. This can't continue,' " she said. "The rest are like, 'Why didn't I buy in 2010 or 2011?' "A separate concern is that low interest rates have distorted prices. Most homeowners who need a mortgage shop for a home based on their monthly payment. The Federal Reserve's policies have kept mortgage rates near all-time lows. While those rates have helped make homes extremely affordable, homes could look much less affordable once mortgage rates rise, especially if prices have zoomed higher.

"What you've got is something other than a sensible market-deciding price. You've got it goosed by the terms of finance, which are extraordinary," said Robert Albertson, chief strategist at Sandler O'Neill + Partners, an investment-banking firm in New York. "Prices shouldn't be up this high, this quickly. It's a big, flapping yellow flag saying we're back in territory that we should not be in."

Market Report: European Stocks Pause After Gains
By Tommy Stubbington and Saabira Chaudhuri
659 words
31 December 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
22
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

European stocks snapped a six-session winning streak Monday, edging down after closing at five-year highs Friday.

The Stoxx Europe 600 Index declined 0.2% to 327.13. Germany's DAX fell 0.4% to 9552.16 after hitting an all-time high Friday. France's CAC 40 slipped 0.05% to 4275.71, and London's FTSE 100 shed 0.3% to 6731.27.

Despite sizable gains in 2013, many investors remain confident that European equities can sustain their run next year.

"Unlike their global peers, European company profits and stock markets remain well below peak," said Kevin Lilley, European equity manager at Old Mutual Global Investors. "With attractive multiples, a recovering economy, a return of international investors and an unlimited European Central Bank backstop in place, Europe would appear to have significant potential for 2014."

Shares also took a breather in the U.S., with the market on track for its best annual performance since the late 1990s. Indexes were mixed after pending home sales ticked up by 0.2% in November, falling short of economists' expectations for 1%, according to the National Association of Realtors.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average added 25.88 points, or 0.2%, to 16504.29, a record high. The S&P 500 fell 0.33 point, or less than 0.1%, to 1841.07. The Nasdaq Composite slipped 2.40 points, or 0.1%, to 4154.20.

Investors said they were wary of making big moves or changing course at the close of a blockbuster year. "Everyone is holding their breath until the end of the year so they can mark their performance," said Ron Florance, deputy chief investment officer at Wells Fargo Private Bank, which manages $170 billion. "It's been such a phenomenal year on the equities side, and the beginning of a painful process for the fixed-income side."

Mr. Florance said he is expecting "a more normal environment" in 2014, with stocks in the U.S. gaining 6% to 8%. He said he favors stocks in the industrial sector due to the pickup in manufacturing activity, as well as stocks in the consumer-discretionary and information-technology sectors. He is pulling back on longer-dated bonds as interest rates rise.

In Asia, most markets rose. Tokyo's Nikkei Stock Average climbed 0.7% on its last day of trading for 2013 to end at 16291.31, its highest close in six years. That sealed a 57% gain for the benchmark this year.

Twitter was down 5.3% in late trade, extending a 13% loss on Friday after an analyst downgraded the microblogging website on concerns that its stock was too expensive. Before the selloff, the stock had surged 76% in December.

Crocs surged 21% after announcing over the weekend that Blackstone Group will invest $200 million in the footwear company, giving Blackstone a 13% ownership stake in Crocs.

The price of the 10-year Treasury note rose 8/32, pushing the yield back below 3% to 2.976%. Over the past few months, buyers have shown up when the 10-year yield approached 3%, limiting the pace of the yield's rise. The yield is a key reference for long-term interest rates for consumers, banks and companies in the U.S. and abroad.

"For money managers who don't have to worry about year-end, there's decent value to be had with 10-year yields right around the 3% mark,"" said Guy LeBas, chief fixed-income strategist at Janney Montgomery Scott LLC in Philadelphia.

Crude for February delivery lost $1.03 a barrel, or 1%, to $99.29 on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold for January delivery fell $11 a troy ounce, or 0.9%, to $1,203.10 on the Comex division of Nymex.

---

Dan Strumpf contributed to this article.

U.S. Pending Home Sales Rise 0.2% in November; Housing Sector Seen Regaining Momentum Lost in Middle of 2013
Jonathan House
659 words
30 December 2013
07:13
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTONA gauge of coming home sales ticked higher in November after several months of declines, a sign that demand for housing is stabilizing after taking a hit from higher interest rates earlier in the year.

The National Association of Realtors said Monday that its seasonally adjusted index of pending sales of existing homes rose 0.2% in November from the prior month to 101.7. The index of 101.7 is against a benchmark of 100, which is equal to the average level of activity in 2001, the starting point for the index.

The November uptick was the first increase since May when the index hit a six-year high, but it was less than the 1% that economists had forecast.

The report was the latest signal that the housing rebound is recovering some of the momentum it lost when soaring prices and rising interest rates pushed some buyers out of the market. Pending home sales are a leading indicator of demand as they tally sales at the moment contracts are signed.

It also adds to the recent stream of data showing a pickup in the economic recovery in the final months of the year. Growth in housing sends positive ripple effects throughout the economy by driving demand for construction materials and bolstering consumer confidence.

"The housing market should continue to find its footing," said Gennadiy Goldberg, a strategist at TD Securities. "We expect [it] to continue its path toward gradual recovery in the coming year," he said.

The National Association of Realtors forecasts total existing-home sales will reach 5.1 million this year, a gain of almost 10% over last year. It expects sales to be at around the same level in 2014 and then rise to 5.3 million in 2015.

Steady employment gains have helped prospective home buyers absorb the impact of higher mortgage rates, while home builders have ramped up construction activity and helped to ease a shortage of homes available for sale. Data released earlier this month by the Commerce Department showed that new home construction in November surged to its highest level in nearly six years.

New supplies can help to moderate rapid price gains in the country's hottest housing markets and encourage new purchases. Prices are back to all-time highs in 10 of the nation's 50 largest metropolitan areas, according to a Wall Street Journal analysis of price data from Zillow, an online real-estate information service.

One potential headwind for the housing sector, rising interest rates, could gain strength in the coming months. Growing confidence in the economy pushed the closely followed yield on the U.S. 10-year Treasury bond above 3% on Friday for the first time in more than two years. The 10-year Treasury yield is a reference point for many loans to U.S. businesses and consumers including mortgage loans.

Also, the Federal Reserve announced earlier this month it will begin winding down an $85 billion monthly bond-buying program intended to hold down rates, spur investment and encourage hiring. When Fed officials first signaled they were thinking about doing so this spring, mortgage rates surged.

Since then the gains have moderated, though rates have continued to trend higher. For the week ended Dec. 26, the 30-year fixed-rate mortgage averaged 4.48%, compared with 4.47% a week earlier and 3.35% a year earlier, according to Freddie Mac. Rates on 15-year fixed-rate mortgages averaged 3.52%, compared with 3.51% the previous week and 2.65% a year earlier.

Each one percentage-point increase in mortgage rates makes homes about 10% more expensive for buyers by increasing the monthly mortgage payments. Rates, however, remain near historical lows, so the prospect of further increases could encourage potential purchasers to make a decision to buy.

MANSION --- Spread Sheet: What Price Beauty? --- A recent study asks whether potential buyers would pay more money for homes staged with attractive decor
By Sanette Tanaka
675 words
27 December 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
M8
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

How much does a tacky purple wall color affect a home's sale price? Not much, according to new research on home staging.

While good staging does influence a home buyer's overall impression of a house, staging alone doesn't result in buyers willing to pay more for the house, says Michael Seiler, professor of real estate and finance at the College of William and Mary, who researched how home buyers responded to six house tours that varied in paint color and furniture quality.

His findings show that buyers are willing to pay roughly $204,000 in each of the house examples, regardless of the quality of furnishings or paint color. However, the research subjects believed that other buyers would adjust their pricing based on how the house is staged.

"We were able to parse out what you consciously believe and subconsciously believe," Mr. Seiler says. "Beforehand, everyone thinks poor staging is going to be a problem. But when we actually did the experiment, we found it doesn't matter."

Mr. Seiler and co-authors Mark Lane of Old Dominion University and Vicky Seiler of Johns Hopkins University led 820 home-buyers through one of six virtual house tours in March 2012. Using professional-grade rendering software created by an architecture firm in Virginia Beach, Va., each house featured either a "neutral" beige wall color or an "unattractive" purple paint color, and "good" furniture, "ugly" furniture or no furniture. The neutral and attractive options were chosen to appeal to the greatest number of people, Mr. Seiler says. The home buyers then reported what they would be willing to pay, as well as their overall impression of the house.

Still, Mr. Seiler warns: "All we could test is how much the home would sell for. What we don't know is whether a well-staged home will sell faster. It may sell quicker."

The study, "The Impact of Staging Conditions on Residential Real Estate Demand," has been accepted by the Journal of Housing Research for publication sometime next year, he said.

It may be hard to persuade real-estate professionals of the findings.

Doug Eichman, a real-estate agent with Core in New York City, spent more than $30,000 to stage a Midtown East penthouse co-op listed for $6.995 million. His stager, Cheryl Eisen, president of New York City-based Interior Marketing Group Inc., says staging works when buyers feel emotionally connected to the house.

"The bare-bones reason for staging initially is to show buyers how they can function in a space," Ms. Eisen says. "When you go over the top, you make them have an emotional reaction to the space. If they fall in love with the space, they will be willing to pay for it."

Darci Willis, a real-estate agent with Century 21 Scheetz in Carmel, Ind., says that when potential buyers are on the fence, a well-staged home may be a deciding factor.

"Even though people logically know that they can change the paint color, it can be distracting and off-putting. Buyers are thinking emotionally at that point," she says.

---

Ugly Study

Researchers took 820 home buyers through one of six virtual tours of homes that varied in furnishings and wall color. Respondents then rated their impressions of the house and said what they would be willing to pay for it. The findings:

-- Respondents said they would pay roughly $204,000 for the home in all six of the examples, regardless of the quality of furnishings or paint color.

-- Respondents believed other buyers would pay $7,595.75, or 3.7%, less for a house with an unattractive wall color and $8,297.22, or 4%, less for a house with poor furnishings.

Source: Lead author Michael Seiler, professor of real estate and finance at the College of William and Mary

The ranch is being sold by Mr. Maclay's lender, MLIC Asset Holdings, part of the insurance company MetLife. Located in the Bitterroot Valley outside Missoula, the 2,660-acre property has a working cattle operation, but it is a "kind of a different animal" from most ranches, said listing agent Bill McDavid of Hall and Hall.

"It's got the ski runswe don't see that every day," he added. Met Life declined to comment.

The runs are on the western edge of the ranch, where the property meets the Bitterroot mountain range, Mr. McDavid said. There aren't ski lifts, but whoever buys the property could access the trails with a snowcat or snowmobiles, he said. Tim Newhart, a spokesman for Mr. Maclay, said the paths aren't technically ski runs, although they could be used that way.

There is also a roughly 5,000-square-foot, five-bedroom log home, which Mr. Maclay built in 2006, according to Hall and Hall. A barn and other agriculture facilities are on the property, which is leased to a local rancher. Property taxes are estimated at $12,878 annually.

Investing money into his planned Bitterroot Resort, Mr. Maclay in 2005 mortgaged the Montana ranch that had belonged to his family since the late 1800s, according to Mr. Newhart. But after years of widely publicized negotiations with the U.S. Forest Service, the project failed to get the necessary permits. And after his lenders began foreclosure proceedings, Mr. Maclay in 2012 lost the ranch, where he'd grown up, Mr. Newhart said.

Mr. Newhart said the Forest Service decision is being appealed by a local nonprofit group, but "at this point, the destiny of the lower portion of the property is in the hands of Hall and Hall and whoever the next owner is."

Douglas Elliman chairman Howard Lorber, who owns homes in New York City, the Hamptons and Miami, is trading up in Okeechobee, Fla.

Mr. Lorber, who is also chief executive officer of Vector, a company with real estate and tobacco units, said he is listing his 2,400-square-foot, three bedroom, two bathroom cabin in the Pine Creek Sporting Club for $855,000, including furniture, dcor and linens. He's currently constructing a 7,000-square-foot home on over 40 acres in the same resort; it is expected to be completed in the spring.

"At first you say to yourself, 'I want a lower entry point to see how much I'm going to use it'," said Mr. Lorber. He said he spends about 30 days a year in Okeechobee.

Mr. Lorber bought the newly-built and fully furnished cabin in 2010 for about $800,000. On a small lot, it overlooks a lake and has 1,200 square feet of screened-in porch space. Pine Creek, a private hunting resort community, has a swimming pool, spa and fitness center and offers activities like horseback riding and guided hunts. Annual dues are $22,500.

Mr. Lorber's Southampton estate is currently listed for $18.5 million. He said he's downsizing now that his children are grown.

John Reynolds, Pine Creek's director of sales and marketing, is handling the Okeechobee listing.

A Fifth Avenue penthouse owned by the estate of real-estate developer Harvey Auerbach has sold for its full asking price of $14.95 million.

The 3,572-square-foot, three-bedroom, 4 bath apartment is on the 23rd floor of a rare prewar condo at the corner of 86th Street, said listing broker Carol Staab of Douglas Elliman. The full-floor unit has protected views of the Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis Reservoir in Central Park, Ms. Staab said, as well as views of the Empire State and Chrysler buildings and the George Washington Bridge. Included in the purchase was a 290-square-foot staff quarters on the building's third floor and two storage units in the basement.

The founder and chairman of Gold Coast Bank, Mr. Auerbach in the 1970s founded Long Island-based Brookwood Management Company, which owns and manages residential and commercial real estate properties. He bought the apartment in 2010 for $10.95 million, according to Ms. Staab.

Mr. Auerbach's children, Steven Auerbach and Nancy Karwick, now run Brookwood. Ms. Staab said after their father's death in 2012, they considered keeping the apartment but ultimately decided not to. According to public records, the family sold Mr. Auerbach's home in Water Mill, NY for $8.75 million in May.

Ms. Staab declined to name the purchaser of the apartment, but said it sold to an international buyer from Asia who paid all-cash.
