Real Estate
Builders Get Into Jumbos; Pricey new homes are getting easier to finance as builders look for ways to help wealthy borrowers win approval from lenders
AnnaMaria Andriotis
947 words
26 December 2013
12:45
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Wealthy buyers are getting mortgage pitches from an unlikely source: luxury home builders.

Before they have even sold a new home to prospective clients, home builders are directing buyers to their financing arms that have been rolling out generous terms on jumbo mortgages over the past year. Perks include longer interest-rate locks, which is the period borrowers can keep the same rate for until they get the loan, and relatively low down-payment requirements.

The push into jumbo lending follows a surge in new-home sales and prices. Nationwide, 402,000 new homes sold during the first 11 months of the year, up 18.2% from the same period a year prior, according to the U.S. Census Bureau. Average prices were up 17.1% in November compared with a year prior.

But sales in the upper-end of the market represent a sliver of this activity. The most expensive homesthose with a sales price of $750,000 or more, according to the Censusaccounted for just 5% of all sales during November, up from 2% in the same month last year. Builders say a lack of new-home financing for expensive homes is partly to blame. While affluent buyers could pay all-cash, many don't want to lock millions of dollars into a home.

To boost sales, builders are jumping into the jumbo-mortgage market.

Builders say financing referrals are intended to streamline the mortgage application process and to remove obstacles that buyers often encounter. Unlike existing homes, which affluent buyers have few problems getting mortgages for, new properties can be difficult to finance. The problem is often with the home rather than the buyer. Lenders routinely look for red flags, like whether the builder's finances are shaky, or in the case of a new condo, whether too many units remain unsold in that building.

In contrast, builders' financing arms have approved the property as have the banks that they refer buyers to. Qualifying applicants should have the loan in about half the time (four to six weeks) than what most lenders would require. And because the builder and lender are connected, they can update each other on factors that may impact the mortgage.

To be sure, after the housing boom, regulators alleged in some cases that close ties between builders and loan officers (among other parties) had resulted in buyers paying more for services. Stephen Melman, director of economic services for the National Association of Home Builders, says those practices have come to an end and that builders are trying to avoid situations where financing unexpectedly falls through.

In today's market, some builders are rolling out loan features similar to those at regular banks. Last month, TBI Mortgage Co., the financing arm of home builder Toll Brothers, extended its rate lock offer for jumbos to 270 days, up from 120 days. This month, TBI began permitting lower down payments of 10% to 15% (down from a minimum of 20%) for mortgages of up to $1 million, though those borrowers will have to sign up for private mortgage insurance.

Jumbo loans accounted for 19.2% of all TBI's mortgage originations from January through October, up from 11.2% the same period last year, according to the company.

Stephen Wilson, CFO of London Bay Homes, a luxury home builder in Florida, says roughly 30% of its buyers in Naples, where its home prices start at $1 million, sign up for financing. In Sarasota, it is roughly half. The company alerts buyers to four lenders that it says have deep knowledge of the high-end market in those areas.

At the Ritz-Carlton Residences in Lake Tahoe, where condominiums starting at $1.5 million went on sale this year, four of the 11 units that sold so far were financed, says Stuart Cramer, president of Kennedy Wilson Residential Investments Group, a real-estate firm that is a managing partner for this property. Mr. Cramer says the sales team suggests lenders that can provide financing within 30 days.

With small builders, financing can exist in one-off deals. For instance, RR Builders in New Canaan, Conn., has been trying to unload an 11,000-square-foot six-bedroom mansion since 2009. Managing director Rich Rosano says he began offering a bridge loan for the property, whose price tag is $6.1 million, after potential buyers mentioned they wanted to sell their existing home first so that they wouldn't have two mortgages simultaneously.

Buyers should consider shopping around for a mortgage before engaging with a builder. Here are other points to consider:

Higher interest rates: Builders' financing armswhich tend to pull quotes from regular banks and then sell the loans they originatemay not always offer the lowest rates. Separately, lenders' affiliates may be less inclined to give the applicant the lowest rate since they don't have a history with them.

Financing sources: Buyers should ask builders' financing arms how many lenders they are pulling quotes from. Some will act like mortgage brokers going to several banks, including small and large lenders, for rates.

Perks without financing: Buyers should ask builders if they discount their mortgage costseven if they don't have a financing arm. Roughly 27% of builders say they offer to pay closing costs and 7% say they will pay pointsoptional fees paid upfront that lower the loan's interest rate, according to an October survey of roughly 500 builders conducted by the National Association of Home Builders.

Dollar Up Against Yen, Euro; U.S. economic numbers indicate recovery continues to gain traction

The dollar rose against the yen and the euro in a thinly traded preholiday market Tuesday, boosted by higher-than-expected U.S. economic numbers that showed the recovery continues to gain traction.

Demand for U.S. durable goods saw its strongest gain last month since May, while new home sales continued at a brisk pace, rising nearly 17% from a year ago, government data showed.

While the dollar strengthened against its peers on the news, gains were limited, with trading volumes low as most market participants already are on vacation break.

U.S. Goods Orders, New-Home Sales Point to Growth; Excluding Transportation, Manufactured-Goods Orders Make Strongest Gain Since May
Sarah Portlock
731 words
24 December 2013
07:19
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTONA pair of economic reports released Tuesday showed renewed optimism by businesses and prospective homeowners, two of the biggest drivers of U.S. economic growth.

Orders for U.S. durable goodsbig-ticket items such as cars and aircraft designed to last more than three yearsrose by 3.5% last month, reversing a decline in October, the Commerce Department said. Excluding the volatile transportation category, manufactured-goods orders rose 1.2%, the strongest gain since May.

Meanwhile, Americans continued to purchase new homes at a brisk pace in November, the latest sign the housing market is regaining traction after a rise in mortgage rates. New-home sales hit a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 464,000 last month, down only 2.1% from October's upwardly revised annual rate of 474,000, the Commerce Department said in a separate report. October and November marked the two strongest months of new-home sales since mid-2008, during the recession.

The reports point to an economy on stronger footing as the year winds to a close, with businesses picking up investment and new home sales remaining near postrecession peaks.

"It's a holly, jolly data Christmas for the economy as all signs point to an accelerating economy," said Joel Naroff, chief economist at Naroff Economic Advisors.

The data led another firm, Macroeconomic Advisers, to raise its estimate for fourth-quarter growth. It now forecasts gross domestic product to expand at an annualized rate of 2.6% in the final three months of the year, up three-tenths of a percentage point from an earlier estimate.

The overall durable goods increase was driven by business investment, particularly in civilian aircraft orders, which rose nearly 22%. But a broader measure of business spending on software and equipment rose at a solid pace in November after falling in recent months. Orders for nondefense capital goods, excluding aircraft, increased by 4.5%, its strongest pace since January. That could be a sign businesses stepped up spending after the partial government shutdown in October.

Weak business spending has weighed down the U.S. recovery, with many companies pointing to uncertainty from Washington as a reason for hesitation. The two-year budget agreement reached in Congress earlier this month may quell some of those concerns, however.

"The tone of durable goods orders improved substantially in November," said TD Securities strategist Gennadiy Goldberg. "Stronger orders bode well for both the fourth quarter and 2014 economic growth prospects, suggesting that companies remain keen on increasing investment and expanding capacity as the economy and consumer sentiment improve."

Another bright spot was stronger demand for motor vehicles last month. Orders in the category rose 3.3%, the strongest since February. That indicates consumers are once again buying cars after delaying the decision in recent years.

Meanwhile, the new-home sales report suggests the housing market is picking back up after a rise in mortgage rates dented sales earlier this year. Mortgage rates climbed more than a percentage point starting in the spring after Federal Reserve officials signaled they were considering reining in the central bank's $85 billion-a-month bond-buying program. The higher borrowing costs, coupled with a rise in home prices, likely scared off some prospective buyers. But households may be adjusting to the higher rates, which are still historically low but climbing.

Because new-home sales are tallied at the signing of a contract rather than at the closing, they can be an early indicator of housing-market trends.

From a year ago, new-home sales were up 16.6% in November.

The monthly pace of new-home sales caused inventory to decline, the Commerce Department said. At November's pace, it would take 4.3 months to sell homes on the market, down from 4.5 months in October.

Still, underlying demand in the U.S. remains sluggish compared with past recoveries, with much of the third-quarter growth due to companies restocking inventories. Unemployment remains at an elevated 7% with nearly 11 million unemployed workers. Consumer and business spending, while showing healthy increases lately, are still at sluggish levels. And workers' wages are growing only tepidly, raising concerns about how long consumers will be able to maintain the current level of spending.

New York
Bloomberg Reshaped the City; Leaves Sprawling Legacy as He Winds Down His 12 Years as the Mayor of New York City
2927 words
23 December 2013
16:26
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Crime dropped, high-school graduation rates rose and luxury residential towers re-etched the skyline. Michael Bloomberg leaves a sprawling legacy as he winds down his 12 years as the mayor of New York City.

A billionaire businessman who took office with no experience in government, Mr. Bloomberg won election three times, beginning just weeks after the Sept. 11, 2001, terror attacks. He guided the city through its toughest economic times in decades.

In emergencies and their aftermaths, Mr. Bloomberg won accoladesfor his handling of a blackout in 2003 and superstorm Sandy that struck the city last year. But he took sharp criticism for the city's slow response to a 2010 post-Christmas snowstorm.

Mr. Bloomberg, a generous philanthropist, unveiled a number of initiatives to fight poverty in New York. Still, on his watch, the number of homeless people in city shelters rose. Sometimes, his policies seem to reach everywhereor tried to. He banned smoking at bars, restaurants, parks and beaches. He wanted to ban large, sugary drinks, but the courts ruled against him.

For all Mr. Bloomberg's successes, some of his highest-profile initiatives failed, including his pursuit of the Summer Olympic Games. But the mayor and his aides have said those battles were worth waging, the mark of a leader who didn't back away from difficult challenges.

Mr. Bloomberg brought a rigorous market sensibility when he gained control of the nation's largest school system in 2002. Parents were urged to shop around and choose the best school for their children. The city put high-school admissions into overdrive, allowing students to apply to up to 12 schools.

The mayor also set about opening new schoolsmany of which shared buildingsultimately increasing the number by more than 40%. That included charter schools. The mayor took the position that charters were city schools too, because they taught city residents with public dollars, even though they weren't part of the Bloomberg administration.

A City Hall philosophy grounded in the principles of competition and choice drove much of the policy-making. There was a relentless reliance on using test-score data to grade teachers, principals and schools. Administration officials pushed for transparency, such as the public release of teacher ratings, when they believed it would spur competition.

But the side effects of Mr. Bloomberg's policies became flashpoints for his critics.

The focus on test scores, coupled with an increase in testing from the state, led to backlash from parents, teachers and principals. They said schools were turning into factories that prepped students for testsand little else.

An A through F letter grading system instituted in 2007 for schools was intended as a handy reference to help families cut through the complex data on test scores, graduation rates and coursework and allow students to gravitate toward the better schools. In many cases, that is what happened, but the worst schools were knocked into a downward spiral.

As Mr. Bloomberg sought to reshape the schools, academic results improved, for the most part. Graduation rates are the mayor's clearest success: The graduation rate has hovered around 60% for the past few years, up from 46.5% in 2005. On state tests, city schools have soared compared to schools in the rest of the state.

A federal test that measures progress in cities, however, showed the city hadn't improved nearly as much as the average of other large cities. Asked to explain, schools officials said the city's test scores were already so much higher than average that it was harder to improve.

As befits a mayor captivated by measurable data, statisticssome widely lauded and others the subject of fierce debatedefine the Bloomberg era when it comes to fighting crime.

With homicides this year standing at 329 through Monday, the city is on a pace to record the fewest killings since the 1950s. Crime and the number of shootings have both decreased 31% since Mr. Bloomberg took office and appointed Raymond Kelly to head the New York Police Department. The reduction came about even as the NYPD lost thousands officers over Mr. Bloomberg's years in office and as the department confronted the heightened threat of terror.

"It's a remarkably strong legacy," said Richard Aborn, president of the Citizens Crime Commission of New York City. "They have driven crime to rates no one thought was possible. Every time it went lower people said it can't go down any further but it has, particularly murders."

But other numbers also will shape Mr. Bloomberg's legacy: the more than 5 million stop-and-frisk reports filled out by police, more than 80% of them involving black and Hispanic men, of whom about 90% weren't charged.

Critics see it as a black mark on the Bloomberg years. "One of the areas in which the administration crashed and burned particularly in the last half of his third term is on the issue of stop and frisk," said Vince Warren, executive director for the Center for Constitutional Rights. "What will be remembered about his administration is that it fought tooth and nail to continue a failed police policy with respect to crime reduction that hurt far more New Yorkers than it helped."

In August, a federal judge ruled the policy, as carried out by the NYPD, was racially biased and unconstitutional. The judge also ordered an outside monitor to be installed at the NYPD. The city is appealing, but Mayor-elect Bill de Blasio has vowed to drop the appeal once in office.

Under Mr. Bloomberg, the city has also made a record number of misdemeanor arrestsan annual average of 220,000 between 2002 and 2012including for possession of small amounts of marijuana.

Critics, such as John Jay College of Criminal Justice sociology professor Andrew Karmen, say these relatively minor charges, many of which were the result of stop and frisk, can have lifelong effects on education and job opportunities for the primarily black and Hispanic men arrested.

Nonetheless, Mr. Aborn said, he suspected that "in the long view of history the reduction of crime will be dominate."

After all the fuss, the number might not sound so big: 39 acres.

That is the total area of New York City's vehicle-choked streets since 2007 that has been converted into pedestrian plazas, public seating, or expanded crosswalks and medians, according to a Nov. 21 report from the city's Department of Transportation.

That area, plus 141 acres of bike lanes (350 miles), comprise the alterations made by Mr. Bloomberg and his transportation department to the New York City streetscape.

Green paint formed the bike lanes, and thermoplastic divider markings blowtorched onto pavement demarcated the protected areas for walkers, bikers and those not driving cars. It was quick and cheap, in terms of the transportation infrastructure.

And often the alterations were small. For every headline-grabbing pedestrian plaza, such as driving lanes reserved for pedestrian use and recreation at Times Square and Herald Square, the transportation department worked at dozens of lesser known sites. Using its own lexicon, the department installed "neckdowns" and "bulbouts" at intersections to extend pedestrian-only areas, making it easier to cross the street.

In the Nov. 21 report, the DOT said it had improved design to enhance safety on 137 "street corridors," and done the same at 113 intersections citywide.

City traffic fatalities have fallen 30% since 2001, the department said. And the city, like some in the business community, says the emphasis on creating public spaces has been good business. Retail rents have doubled in Times Square since the creation of the plazas there in 2009, Mr. Bloomberg has said.

Challengesand dissentersremain. Years after supporters declared victory, a legal battle drags on over the bike lane along tony Prospect Park West in Brooklyn. Some drivers, including taxi drivers, continue to grumble about the loss of driving lanes.

"Nothing happens in New York without vigorous conversation and some degree of contention," said DOT Commissioner Janette Sadik-Khan in the DOT report last month, "but all indicators, from the heavy use of new space and travel options to the number of applications and requests for projects and survey after survey of New Yorkers' views of our policies, shows very high support."

From atop One57, the new 1,004-foot glass tower in Midtown, where two penthouses are in contract for $90 million or more, New York City is a very different place than it was when Mr. Bloomberg took office in 2002.

Tall apartment buildings now soar in once-industrial neighborhoods on the West Side. New towers have sprouted along the decayed waterfront in Williamsburg and many other places.

It amounted to the biggest building boom in a generation.

The Bloomberg years ushered in a new vision for condo living and renewed interest in architecture and design by developers and the public alike. Glass-walled buildings with high ceilings and skyscraper views outsold older co-ops lining Central Park.

Buyers from around the world rushed in to buy a piece of New York, bidding up prices on apartments and houses. With falling crime rates across the city, neighborhoods in Brooklyn became a destination of choice, for many newcomers to the city.

After a sharp drop following the financial crisis in 2008, New York real estate rebounded more strongly and quickly than the rest of the country, said Pamela Liebman, the president of Corcoran Group, a large residential brokerage. "It has been the most fruitful time in history to be in the real-estate business," she said.

City records show building permits for 239,000 homes were issued during the Bloomberg years through the end of October, though some projects were delayed during the downturn. That was more than any comparable period since the city's fiscal crisis in the mid-1970s.

Yet the city grew even faster. The labor force rose 9.7%. between 2000 and 2010, census data show. The result: Higher rents and reduced affordability, as New Yorkersespecially lower income peoplepaid a rising share of their income on rents and utilities. In his first term, Mr. Bloomberg announced a plan to expand affordable housing by harnessing private investment, at a cost that will eventually reach about $8.5 billion. The city now says that 50,111 affordable apartments have been constructed, and 107,119 below-market units were saved to date.

"Mayor Bloomberg moved mountains," said Vicki Been, who studies housing issues at New York University. She said that the challenge for Mayor-elect Bill de Blasio was to improve affordability of housing, when incomes are stalled and rents are high.

Over the past dozen years, the city has spent more than $2 billion on renovation and construction for museums, theaters and other venues. The physical footprint of nearly every of New York's major cultural institutions has been transformed.

The $2 billion includes projects initiated before Mr. Bloomberg took officenotably, the overhaul of the Lincoln Center campus and the Brooklyn Academy of Music Cultural District. But arts leaders say Mr. Bloomberg nevertheless deserves credit for those complex projects because his administration coordinated agencies and cut through red tape to make them a reality.

Mr. Bloomberg and his chief arts advisersFirst Deputy Mayor Patricia Harris and Cultural Affairs Commissioner Kate Levinsaw the arts as a driver for tourism and economic development.

The administration also eased the way for a range of splashy public-art projects, from Christo and Jeanne-Claude's "The Gates" in Central Park and Olafur Eliasson's 90-to-120-foot waterfalls on the East River to a living room built around the statue at Columbus Circle.

At the same time, Mr. Bloomberg has been criticized for allowing school principals to use, at their discretion, funding that under Mayor Rudolph Giuliani had been earmarked for arts. The policy is one reason why the city doesn't comply with state guidelines on arts instruction. City officials have said the decision gave principals the power to make the best decisions about resources. The number of arts groups working with city schools increased 31%, to 497, in 2011-12 when compared with the previous year.

One major project initiated in the Bloomberg years was the new Whitney Museum of American Art, scheduled to open in 2015 at the south end of the elevated High Line park (which also was developed during the Bloomberg years). The city committed $55 million for the Whitney's new $422 million home.

Another project, Culture Shed, an exhibition and event space planned for the Hudson Yards development on Manhattan's west side, is intended to anchor the north end of a new cultural corridor along the High Line. The city last week committed another $25 million to the project, bringing the total to $75 million. Some arts groups criticized the city for allocating so much money to a new organization rather than using the money to support those that already exist.

Others praised the Bloomberg administration for thinking big. "Having a bold vision is part of who they are," said Karen Brooks Hopkins, president of the Brooklyn Academy of Music.

A spirited, multipronged battle against obesity emerged as one of Mr. Bloomberg's most talked-about themes in public-health policy.

The mayor banned trans-fats in restaurants, mandated calorie counts and unleashed a blitz of public-service campaigns to curb obesity.

The initiatives sometimes sent eyes rollingand sometimes died in the courtsbut public-health advocates said they drove impressive gains. Obesity rates for New York City children dipped over the Bloomberg years, and they rose for adults, but overall they remained well below national rates.

"We've started to make some progress but we have a long way to go," said Thomas Farley, commissioner of the city's Department of Health. "It's not happening fast; it's a small decline. But it's very exciting."

Top researchers and physicians who study obesity say the policies work, and they credit the mayor with turning the issue into a national priority. "Many of those things have had a profound impact on the life expectancy of New Yorkers," said Dr. Andrew Racine, the senior vice president and chief medical officer at Montefiore Medical Center, whose research focuses on the impact of public policy on health outcomes.

Critics suggested that some of the mayor's public-health policies, including a ban on large, sugary drinks that was ruled illegal, stigmatized New Yorkers in poverty.

Joel Berg, executive director of the New York City Coalition Against Hunger, said the mayor should have been as public about fighting hunger as obesity.

"There is plenty of data that says there are underweight kids and stunted kids in this city but you never hear him talk about that," he said.

Anti-poverty advocates also say that while the mayor helped increase access to healthier food in poor neighborhoods, some of his policies failed to appreciate the relationship between hunger, poverty and obesity. Researchers say hungry people living in poverty are more likely to turn to food that is high in calories but low in nutrition.

Mr. Farley said that criticism was unfair, because solving the problem requires a change in national food policy. "We have taken that very seriously," he said. "Have we solved it? No. But there are some very large economic forces that are out of our control."

In January, Mr. Bloomberg stood shoulder-to-shoulder with John Rhea, chairman of the public-housing authority, and promised to eliminate a backlog of some 420,000 repairs at the agency's buildings.

They aren't expected to meet that goal by the end of the mayor's term, but the number has dwindled to about 115,000 pending repair orders as of last week, according to data provided by the New York State Housing Authority. Officials say only 90,000 is achievable because they receive new calls every day.

The backlog was the most tangible symptom of the financial crisis facing the agency. It has an annual budget shortfall of $60 million along with $16 billion of unfunded capital improvements over the next five years to things such as roofs, bricks, elevators and heating systems.

While Mr. Bloomberg has been praised for his innovative approaches to problems that had long vexed New York, running the lumbering public-housing demanded more nuts-and-bolts solutions that ultimately seemed to elude his administration.

To be sure, the agency also faced challenges beyond the mayor's control. Since 2001, federal funding to the agency has fallen by about $2 billion.

But State Assemblyman Brian Kavanagh and others said the city could have significantly helped the agency by eliminating some $100 million it pays each year for policing, sanitation and payments in lieu of taxes.

"I think he's failed NYCHA at a critical time in its history," said Victor Bach, a senior housing-policy analyst at the Community Service Society of New York.

The administration moved to shore up the agency's finances earlier this year, when it announced a plan to lease eight parcels of land to private developers, generating $30 million to $50 million. After an outcry from tenant groups and politicians that the agency moved too quickly with little consultation, Mr. Rhea said the authority heard those concerns and won't select developers before the end of Mr. Bloomberg's term.

MANSION --- The Market: The New Taste for Townhouses --- In New York, international buyers are snubbing high-price, high-rise condominiums and opting for historic brownstones; financial scrutiny is less stringent but snow-shoveling isn't included
By Candace Taylor
1244 words
20 December 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
M5
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Waves of international buyers are causing a sea change in Manhattan's luxury property market.

Affluent out-of-towners are starting to spurn glitzy, full-service condo buildings as prices reach upward of $90 million, opting instead to buy older townhouses, which are seen as a better value, real-estate agents say.

Brian Lewis, a broker at Halstead Property in Manhattan, says he put a historic Charlton Street townhouse on the market in the spring for $7.25 million and was inundated with inquiries from buyers in Russia, South America and Asia, many of whom planned to use the house as a pied-a-terre. "Usually when I get a call and it's a foreign buyer, I'm pulling out my new development Rolodex," says Mr. Lewis, who later lowered the price to $6.25 million. "The palate of our foreign buyers is changing before our eyes."

The city's townhouses, many of which were built in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, have a reputation for being difficult to maintain. In addition to regular upkeep, tasks like show-shoveling and package pickup typically fall to the homeowner. As a result, historic brownstones have largely been shunned by pied-a-terre buyers and other purchasers who don't plan to use them as a primary residence.

"People look at New York and think of modern conveniences," says Robert Dankner, of Prime Manhattan Residential. "But I have seen more of an emerging trend with buyers who don't live here full time embracing the townhouse market." He is representing a client from Switzerland who is in the process of buying a West Village townhouse for around $10.5 million.

The shift has picked up steam in recent months, says Kevin Royer, an agent at Halstead who is working with an Israeli buyer looking to purchase a townhouse in Brooklyn. "Since this summer, maybe since April, people are suddenly going crazy."

Contributing to the trend is condo boards' increasing scrutiny of buyers' finances. Many boards now ask for extensive income verification from buyers -- even all-cash buyers, says Halstead agent Sarah Parsons. "It's almost like a co-op board," she says. Many of her European buyers, who aren't used to revealing financial information, are now choosing townhouses. "They don't want to disclose what they make," Ms. Parsons says.

Price per square foot and perceived investment value are the other main motivators, brokers say. "More and more, the tendency is to go toward a full townhouse purchase because they can be anonymous, and they can get all the space for a fraction of the price," says Adie Kriegstein, an agent at Core who recently represented a French family in the purchase of a townhouse on West 77th Street.

The average price per square foot of a Manhattan townhouse in the third quarter of 2013 was $1,144, compared with $1,379 for condos, according to data by Miller Samuel Real Estate Appraisers & Consultants. At the high end of the market, the price gap widens. A condo penthouse at 15 Central Park West set a record when it closed last year for $88 million, or more than $13,000 per square foot. The priciest townhouse sale on record in the city, by contrast, is the 21,700-square-foot Harkness Mansion on the Upper East Side, which was bought by private-equity investor J. Christopher Flowers in 2006 for $53 million, or around $2,440 per square foot.

Jonathan Miller, chief executive of Miller Samuel, points out that comparisons between new construction condos and resale townhouses are somewhat flawed, especially because brownstones don't have the doormen or views usually found in tall towers. Still, buyers have noticed the price disparity.

Ms. Kriegstein's French client, Cecile Caer, says price was the primary factor in her family's decision to opt for a townhouse. Ms. Caer, 43, says she and her husband, an advertising executive, looked at several condos, including one they liked at the Apthorp on the Upper West Side, but decided that a townhouse offered the best value and potential return on investment. They bought a 5,000-square-foot home for $5.2 million, or just over $1,000 per square foot. "We looked at the townhouse and thought: We can do a beautiful job renovating, have all the space we want and still be at about the same price as buying a condo," says Ms. Caer. The trade-off is that the home needs a major renovation, which likely won't be completed until 2015. But she says she feels confident that for "the price you buy versus the price you can eventually sell, the townhouse was the best investment."

Ms. Caer says she doesn't yet know how much time her family will spend in Manhattan -- they also have homes in France and Canada -- but she plans on hiring a property manager or housekeeper to check on the home while they are away.

While condo fees in some New York buildings are thousands of dollars a month, Halstead's Ms. Parsons says hiring a property manager or part-time super can cost $200 to $500 per month. And with a proliferation of property-management firms and new technology such as virtual doormen, taking care of homes from afar is easier than ever.

That was certainly a consideration for Carsten Thoma, a 39-year-old Zurich resident who is looking to buy a multifamily townhouse in Brooklyn. Mr. Thoma, who frequently travels in his role as president of Swiss e-commerce firm Hybris Software, plans to keep one unit for his own use when he's in town, and rent out the others. He won't be in New York "a huge chunk of the year," but that doesn't bother him. "You can easily create a similar experience" to a full-service condo, he says, since there are "concierge services left and right. New York is a super services-oriented city."

Townhouses also come without the monthly common charges that condo owners pay. "We have a lot of international buyers who look at townhouses because they don't want to have to deal with maintenance fees that are very high, and you're not there," says Michelle Bourgeois, an agent at Town Residential. "You can have somebody maintain the townhouse and still come out ahead."

The new interest in townhouses has started to affect prices. Jed Garfield, head of Leslie J. Garfield Real Estate, which specializes in Manhattan townhouses, estimates that during the past two years, prices have grown about 20% per year, compared with 3% to 4% a year before the financial crisis. "The last couple of years, it's really jumped," he says.

The median sale price of a Manhattan luxury condo was $5.25 million in the third quarter, compared with $4.32 million for townhouses, according to Miller Samuel. But townhouses have shown more appreciation over the past six years, with the median price increasing 12.9%, compared with 11.1% for luxury condos. For luxury townhouses, the median sale price jumped 20.4%, from $15.2 million to $18.3 million, between 2009 and 2013.

Construction Boosts Colombia's Economy; Growth Exceeds Expectations in Third Quarter, As Building Sector Jumps 21%
Dan Molinski
441 words

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

BOGOTAThe Colombian economy grew much faster than expected last quarter as construction spending soared and the agriculture and mining sectors rose at a healthy pace, according to data released by the government Thursday.

Economic expansion in the third quarter was 5.1% compared with the same quarter a year earlier, the government statistics agency DANE said. That is the strongest quarterly growth rate since the first quarter of 2012 and beats the average 4.3% expansion rate economists were expecting.

Compared with the second quarter, the economy grew 1.1% in the third quarter, which works out to an annualized rate of 4.5%.

Activity in the construction sector jumped 21% last quarter compared with the third quarter of 2012, while agriculture grew 6.6% and mining and oil rose 6.1%. On the negative side, the manufacturing sector continued to suffer, registering a 1.0% contraction last quarter.

Colombian Finance Minister Mauricio Cardenas earlier this week predicted the third-quarter growth figure would beat expectations, after he saw some preliminary data.

Colombia's oil-driven economy experienced a slowdown in late 2012 and early 2013, leading many economists to predict it would continue to slide, following the path of neighboring Brazil. But the deceleration was rather short-lived as the government began investing heavily in infrastructure spending, and a growing middle class that has continued to increase its spending habits amid a falling unemployment rate.

Still, there are lingering concerns about the health of Colombia's economy. Despite the strong economic data, the country's stock market is 11% lower this year, while the country's currency has fallen about 10% in 2013. Also, this year has seen a wave of popular street marches and violent protests by farmers and other labor groups that say stronger economic growth data isn't trickling down to help the poorer segments of society.

The strong growth data is likely to dissuade Colombia's central bank, whose board members meet Friday for the year's final meeting, from cutting interest rates that are already near an all-time low.

The bank board had left its key rate at 3.25% for eight straight months but decades-low consumer inflation, which is at 1.76% through the past 12 months, led some board members to propose one more rate cut to increase bank borrowing to spur demand. But the data out Thursday suggests more monetary stimulus isn't necessary.

The data also revised down second-quarter on-year growth to 3.9% from 4.2%.

U.K. House Prices Set to Pick Up; The Royal Institute of Chartered Surveyors Forecasts an 8% Rise in 2014
Ilona Billington
735 words
18 December 2013
14:01
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

U.K. house prices are set to rise rapidly in 2014, keeping alive concerns about the threat of a fresh property market bubble that could derail the economic recovery.

The government and regulators have said they don't believe the U.K. is in the early stages of a bubble, and have pledged to take any steps needed should the warning signs grow louder.

Last month, the government and the Bank of England said that a scheme that was intended to encourage banks to provide mortgage loans would be withdrawn. That move is expected to have little impact on prices, which economists expect to rise much more rapidly than the annual rate of inflation and real incomes.

The Royal Institution of Chartered Surveyors Thursday said it expects house prices to rise 8% over 2014 as demand for homes continues to exceed supply.

The group, which estimates house prices grew 5% this year, said that London will likely continue to lead house price growth, although not by as much as it does now, as employment growth and low mortgage rates, together with the government's flagship Help to Buy Scheme, support affordability.

"The cost of a house is now picking up right across the country and next year should see more of the same," said Peter Bolton-King, the RICS global residential director.

"This is largely down to the fact that buyer numbers considerably outweigh the amount of homes on the market. While the number of new homes being built is now on the rise, it still won't be anywhere near enough to meet demand and we expect the problem of insufficient housing stock to be the main driver behind price increases over the next 12 months," Mr. Bolton-King said.

The RICS forecast is a little higher than the average of 6.3% The Wall Street Journal has calculated by combining 13 economists and house builders' forecasts for house price inflation in 2014.

The average reflects a number of views, including the likelihood that the U.K. Treasury and Bank of England's recent decision to withdraw the Funding for Lending Scheme from new mortgages could lead to an increase in average mortgage interest rates.

The program, which offered lenders access to cheaper funding costs if they increased mortgage and business lending, was amended three weeks ago.

"I think we've seen mortgage rates trough, and can't really expect them to fall much lower now that the FLS has been changed to focus on business lending," said Melanie Bowler, U.K. economist for Moody's Analytics.

Moody's Analytics forecasts house-price inflation of 3.6% in 2014, which is a smaller increase than this year.

While some increase in mortgage rates could have an impact on the wider U.K. housing market, London buyers are often overseas investors who don't require a mortgage. As a result, it is likely that prices in the capital will continue to rise more rapidly than elsewhere and remain the focus of concerns about a possible bubble.

The Home Builders Federation, an umbrella group representing the industry in England and Wales, on Wednesday said it had a commitment from 11 builders to offer new homes to U.K. buyers before, or at the same time, as marketing them overseas.

"Whilst opposing further intervention in the market that could reduce housing supply, the Home Builders Federation recognizes the need to ensure U.K. buyers have adequate opportunity to buy the homes the industry is building," the HBF said in a statement.

Asking prices for London properties rose 10.4% over the course of 2013 and hit a record high of 544,232 ($886,119) in October, according to online estate agency Rightmove. That is almost double the 5.4% increase reported across the whole of the U.K. this year.

Chancellor of the Exchequer George Osborne says fears of a house price bubble are overplayed.

"Of course we have to be vigilant and we do now have a system for that vigilance but there is not that [bubble] issue today," Mr. Osborne told lawmakers last week.

The Office for Budget Responsibility, the independent body which assesses the U.K.'s public finances, has forecast U.K. house price inflation of 5.2% next year.

China Property Prices Rising, Developers Falling; Home Prices Gain for 10th Month; Unsold Homes Accumulate
Alex Frangos
475 words
18 December 2013
16:29
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

China's housing prices keep on keeping on. The same can't be said for the country's property developers.

Defying a grab bag of government efforts to cool the market, average prices of new homes in 70 cities rose for the 10th consecutive month in November, increasing 9% from the year earlier, according to government data. The gains remain broad based: 66 of 70 cities surveyed showed price rises in month-over-month terms. There were tentative signs of a high-water mark, as the pace of the increases slowed.

Shares of Chinese developers haven't gone up in lock step. An S&P index of 52 mainland property companies is down 9% year to date, compared with a 5.5% drop in the Shanghai Composite Index. Often ahead of the game, investors sent shares sharply higher in 2012, in anticipation of the recent home-price resurgence. This year, share prices have reflected a foreboding 2014.

Investors have a right to be jittery given that a mass of unsold inventory is building. There are more than 3 million unsold units held by listed property developers, according to Capital Economics, the highest level on record and a million units more than two years ago.

There are more-fundamental worries afoot. Property has boomed because it is one of the few places to stash savings without being financially disadvantaged by state policy. Beijing's overhaul push could give real estate a run for its money by getting rid of artificially low bank-deposit rates, by giving savers better access to overseas investments, and by boosting the moribund stock market with IPOs.

Another risk is that Beijing follows through on its promise to wean China's economy from cheap credit just as sales dry up. In the past two mini real-estate downturns in 2008 and 2011, developers were rescued by fresh credit that tided them over until sales rebounded.

If the Federal Reserve begins to taper its bond purchases in the U.S. and Beijing is trying to instill discipline, especially with regard to real-estate-heavy shadow banking and interbank finance, raising money could prove more challenging.

Most companies' shares have a heap of negativity baked in. Soho China, Evergrande Real Estate Group and China Vanke trade near the bottom of their historic price-to-book value ranges. That may smell like an opportunity to buy. But with so much pressure on Beijing to temper real-estate mania, property prices will have to fall before a fresh cycle can begin.

TORONTOSales of existing homes in Canada were little changed in November from October, edging down just 0.1%, the country's real-estate trade group said in its monthly report Monday.

Year-over-year, home sales last month climbed 5.9%, the Canadian Real Estate Association said.

Sales activity across the country last month was 3.4% lower than the peak reached in September, which CREA said is further evidence that late-summer and early fall activity likely got a boost from homebuyers with pre-approved mortgages at lower-than-current rates getting into the market before their pre-approvals expired.

The Multiple Listing Home Price Index, weighted to compensate for fluctuations in the composition of sales, was up 4.1% in November from a year earlier, it said.

"The size of year-over-year average price gains continues to reflect the decline in sales activity last year in some of Canada's larger and more expensive markets which caused the national average price to drop at that time," CREA said.

Removing the greater Vancouver and greater Toronto markets, it said the year-over-year increase in national average prices drops to 4.3%.

CREA also provided forecasts for this year and next, noting that annual sales in 2013 will remain near 450,000 units for the sixth year in a row.

Overall, it said sales and prices have been slightly stronger than expected in Western Canada, while sales and pricing in Eastern Canada has generally been softer.

Specifically, it expects sales to reach 458,200 units this year, an increase of just under 1% from 2012. For 2014, it expects sales to climb 3.7% to 475,000 units, with British Columbia projected to post the strongest gain.

Home prices are expected to rise 5.2% to an average of C$382,200 this year, and another 2.5% to C$391,100 next year, CREA said. It expects Alberta to lead the rise next year.

OTTAWA--Bank of Canada Governor Stephen Poloz said Thursday he expects the risks posed to the Canadian economy by weak inflation and strengthening property values to diminish over the next two years.

According to prepared remarks he was set to deliver in Montreal, Mr. Poloz said the central bank's outlook anticipates a so-called soft landing in the housing sector along with an eventual pickup in exports and business investment. Housing prices have rebounded in recent months after a weak spell as people moved up buying plans in the wake of higher borrowing costs, triggered by an anticipated scaling back in the U.S. Federal Reserve's bond-buying program.

"This rotation will relieve tension between low demand and household imbalances," Mr. Poloz said. "Nonetheless, the risks around this base case need to be managed."

He said there is a risk housing prices could continue to climb sharply, and "set the stage for a sharp correction down the road. Such a correction would be a risk to both the Canadian economy and our financial system."

Still, Mr. Poloz said data suggest the pace of household borrowing has moderated, and real-estate investment appears to be on a "more sustainable" track.

In its most recent policy decision, delivered last week, the Bank of Canada maintained its benchmark lending rate at 1%, and suggested the downside risks to inflation "appear to be greater," while risks posed by household imbalances -- which encompasses a combination of high consumer debt and overstretched property values -- had not materially changed. Mr. Poloz said the central bank's rate policy is an effort to balance these two sets of risks.

Annual inflation in Canada has remained well below the central bank's target of 2%, with recent data indicating the all-items consumer price index rose a paltry 0.7% in October. Mr. Poloz said it would take "around two years" for inflation to get back to the 2% target, as the Canadian economy slowly returns to full capacity following the global financial crisis in 2008-09.

Japan's Pensions Put Property Back in Play
By Eleanor Warnock
985 words
11 December 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

TOKYO -- Some of Japan's pension funds are moving into the country's newly hot real-estate market in search of higher profits.

The moves are small and lag behind more significant investments by foreign pension funds. But they are an important change in the ultraconservative stance of Japanese institutional investors, which control a total of $3.7 trillion, the world's second-largest pool of retirement funds after the U.S., according to consultancy Towers Watson & Co.

Japanese pensions have shied away from property and stocked up on domestic bonds since the 1990s, after land prices plummeted in the wake of a financial crisis. Property prices have declined nationwide for 20 of the past 22 years, putting investment portfolios underwater and saddling many companies with bad debt.

This year, though, Japan's real estate is on track for its best year since the 2008 financial crisis, as a brighter economic outlook and flow of easy money from the central bank drives cash into shopping centers, warehouses and office buildings. The volume of commercial-real-estate transactions for the first nine months of the year climbed to 2.84 yen trillion ($27.55 billion), 43% higher than all the transactions for 2012, according to Jones Lang LaSalle Inc. The return on Japanese property during the year through May hit 4.5%, while the return on Japan's benchmark 10-year government bond now hovers around 0.66%.

Overall, 43% of Japanese pension funds now have some real-estate exposure, up from 31% in 2009, according to Japan's Association for Real Estate Securitization. Pension funds now account for 14% of the 10 trillion-plus yen in private-real-estate funds, up from 4.8% in 2006, according to another ARES survey.

At property-fund manager Diamond Realty Management Inc., Chief Executive Takashi Tsuji said he has seen increasing interest from pensions in a new fund started last year and estimates the percentage of such investors in the fund could rise to one-third from a quarter in the next few years. The total value of the fund's investments is expected to double to 100 billion yen by the end of December.

Tsuneo Taguchi, who oversees travel firm JTB Corp.'s 120 billion yen pension fund, said he decided to put money in property funds for the first time this year because he needed to boost returns. For pensioners, he added, investments in physical properties were relatively "easy to understand.""

Mr. Taguchi doubled the fund's allocation to non-stock and bond assets to 20% from April and put roughly a third of that into private real-estate funds.

Such tentative moves could get bolder if the 120 yen trillion Government Pension Investment Fund -- the world's biggest pool of public-pension assets -- starts investing in real estate, as a government-appointed panel recommended last month. In recent months, GPIF officials have toured a warehouse that is included in the assets of a property fund, with an eye toward future investment, people familiar with GPIF's plans said.

GPIF participation in the market "will put the stamp of approval on real-estate investment,"" said Ari Druker, head of corporate finance, north Asia, at real-estate-service provider Jones Lang LaSalle KK.

While any GPIF shift is still likely a few years away, it is clear Japanese pension funds have to invest in a broader range of assets to lift returns.

Most corporate funds hold the bulk of their assets in bonds -- some 44% -- and have very little in so-called alternative assets, with roughly 1% in real estate, according to Japan's Pension Fund Association.

In the U.S., in contrast, pension funds on average invest 5.2% of their portfolios in real estate, according to London-based researcher Preqin. Japanese pension funds are still "batting below their weight," in real estate, said Mr. Druker.

The ultraconservative investment strategy has led Japanese public and private pension funds to earn less than their peers in other countries for six out of the 10 years ended in 2012, the most recent year available, according to the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development. The situation is worsening due to mounting payouts as the population ages and bond yields approach all-time lows.

Foreign pension funds have been quick to jump on this year's Japanese real-estate rebound. In February, Canada's biggest pension-fund manager, the Canada Pension Plan Investment Board, said it would commit an additional $316 million to a $500 million joint venture it started in 2011 with Singapore-listed Global Logistic Properties Ltd. In May, the Canadian pension board closed a more than $400 million co-investment deal for office buildings in Tokyo.

APG, the Netherlands's largest pension manager, with 337 euros billion ($462.23 billion) in assets under management, led a small group of investors to close a 40.8 billion yen deal for Japanese distribution centers -- huge warehouses that also operate as shipment hubs -- in July.

Still, many Japanese pension-fund managers are like auto-parts maker Denso Corp., which won't touch real estate for its 400 billion yen fund. Investment group manager Kengo Torii said that even though Denso's fund likely will expand its 5% allocation to non-stock, non-bond holdings like private equity when it reshuffles its portfolio in 2016, "real estate would be difficult" because of the potential impact of a large loss.

Ichiro Tajima, who manages drug maker Astellas Pharma Inc.'s 97 billion yen pension, which has 2% of its assets in real-estate funds, said that many Japanese pensions also are hampered by lack of experience in the sector, compared with global peers. "Funds don't have the know-how to directly buy properties," he said. "But overseas funds are buying lots."

The Property Report
Luxury Builder Hits Home-Price Limits
By Kris Hudson
674 words
11 December 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Luxury home builder Toll Brothers Inc. caters to affluent customers who typically aren't deterred by price increases. But even the wealthy have their limits.

Toll, whose financial results are closely watched by housing observers as a gauge of the high-end market, on Tuesday reported a 10% decline in sales contracts in recent weeks, which analysts and even the company partly attributed to sticker-shock on the part of consumers. Over the past year, Toll has pushed up its average selling price 21% to $703,000.

"Toll's buyers have more discretionary income, but even they have decided that the pricing pace has been too aggressive," said Vicki Bryan, a senior analyst with credit-rating service Gimme Credit LLC. "This sets the tone for the next two quarters in terms of pricing."

That piece of negative news overshadowed the company's otherwise strong earnings report. The company reported a 65% increase in revenue for its fiscal fourth quarter that ended Oct. 31 compared with a year ago, bringing revenue for the period to $1 billion. The gain was due mainly to price increases.

Meanwhile, Toll's City Living division of urban condominiums and townhomes in Philadelphia, New York, New Jersey and Bethesda, Md., is becoming a larger part of its business, accounting for 6.4% of its revenue in 2013 and a projected 7% in 2014. Profit margins in that division are generally among Toll's highest, exceeding 30%.

On Tuesday, investors and analysts looked past Toll's earnings report and focused instead on the current sales slowdown. On a conference call with investors, Toll executives noted that since Nov. 1, sales are off more than 10% from the year-ago pace, effectively canceling out Toll's 6% gain from the previous quarter.

In addition to the price increases, Toll blamed the slowdown on uncertainty about the federal budget and a one-percentage-point rise in interest rates from May to September. Even though November and December typically are slow sales months, the size of the recent declines caught some investors by surprise.

Trading of Toll's stock started strong on Tuesday morning as investors reviewed its performance from the previous quarter, rising by 3.3% to $34.69. It then faltered as investors learned of its recent sales slowdown, closing at $33.34, down 24 cents, or 0.7%, in 4 p.m. composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange.

But Toll executives are optimistic. "We will, I'm quite confident, stay flat [in sales contracts] through the New Year, because there's just not a lot of action," Toll Chief Executive Douglas Yearley said on the call. "We still feel like pent-up demand is improving, demographics are on our side, affordability is in place, and we're cautiously optimistic about the spring season."

Toll on Tuesday set its forecast for 2014 home deliveries to a range of 5,100 to 6,100 at an average selling price of $670,000 to $720,000. It delivered 4,184 homes in fiscal 2013.

The company reported net income for the fourth quarter of $94.9 million, down 77% from its year-ago income, which was much larger due to a bookkeeping adjustment related to the reversal of a tax allowance made at that time. The tax allowance resulted from Toll writing down the value of its land holdings from 2007 to 2009, which earned it a tax credit to be applied against future earnings. Once it was able to use the tax credit, Toll in late 2012 recorded a $394.7 million gain to reflect the future value of those tax savings.

Factoring out taxes and related adjustments, Toll's pretax income amounted to $150.2 million in its fourth quarter, up from $60.7 million a year earlier.

For the full fiscal year, Toll posted net income of $170.6 million, compared with fiscal 2012 income of $487.1 million.

Brickell and Mortar

Jorge Perez, the Miami condo titan who is rebounding after losing a billion-dollar fortune in the real-estate downturn, is making his biggest bet since the recession.

Mr. Perez's Related Group of South Florida joined with GTIS Partners, a New York private-equity firm, to pay $104 million for one of downtown Miami's last waterfront development sites, a four-acre parcel at 444 Brickell Avenue.

The developers plan to build a three-tower mixed-use development designed by Miami's Arquitectonica and Connecticut's Pelli Clarke Pelli, with 1,200 apartments, a five-star hotel and 200,000 square feet of office and retail space.

The previous owner of the property, a company controlled by investor Sergio Socolsky, borrowed $58.5 million against the property in 2006, but defaulted on the loan three years later, according to loan documents. The property went to special servicer CW Capital Asset Management, which sold it to Related.

The new project, to be dubbed One Brickell, would sit on the opposite side of Brickell Avenue from Mr. Perez's highest-profile project from the housing boom: the Icon Brickell, another three-tower complex. In 2010, Mr. Perez handed over two of the Icon's three towers to his lender in a friendly foreclosure after the Miami real-estate market crashed.

-- Robbie Whelan

Desert Dirt

Arizona is back in the business of selling state land to be developed for single-family homes.

The Arizona State Land Department last week sold a 106-acre parcel in Phoenix at auction to home builder Taylor Morrison Home Corp. for $28.1 million. In October, the state sold 193 acres in Mesa to California investor Jerry Ivy and builder Del Pueblo Communities LLC for $30.2 million.

The two sales were the first by the state to home builders since 2007, before the real-estate and financial crises crushed Arizona's land and home markets. The state's land department, which controls 9.2 million acres of land, looks to sell parcels for the best use and price.

"What we've seen of late in terms of the appraised land values and the interest from home builders is, effectively, prices from 2007 and 2006," Arizona Land Commissioner Vanessa Hickman said in an interview.

The Grand Canyon state currently has nine applications from developers to bring to auction state land totaling nearly 1,500 acres, she said. Most of those parcels likely will be auctioned within nine months. But that is still far less than the 2,319 acres that Arizona auctioned in 2007 for residential development, collecting a cumulative $351.9 million.

-- Kris Hudson

Peering Into the Future

When Moor + South took control of Pier 39 in San Francisco about 30 years ago, the company was looking at holding it for a long time. Its long-term lease for the tourist magnet from the San Francisco Port Commission stretched out about six decades.

But time is ticking by. Just recently, Moor + South had to keep an eye on the end of the lease when it refinanced Pier 39.

The hub of shopping and restaurants recently received a $148 million mortgage from American International Group Inc. But unlike the vast majority of commercial property loans, which typically need to be refinanced every five to 10 years, the loan is self-amortizing and is set to run down to zero in 19 years, says David Sonnenblick, of loan-advisory firm Sonnenblick-Eichner Co. who represented Moor + South on the deal.

The operator wanted to be free of debt in advance of the lease expiration. "We have 30 years remaining on the term, so they didn't want to get a 10-year loan," Mr. Sonnenblick says.

-- Eliot Brown

Housing Agency Will Reduce Mortgage-Loan Limits; FHA will drop the maximum mortgage loan it will guarantee next month
Nick Timiraos
584 words
6 December 2013
12:17
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The Federal Housing Administration will drop the maximum size of home-mortgage loans that it will guarantee beginning next month in nearly 650 counties, the agency said Friday.

The maximum for single-family homes in certain "high-cost" housing markets including Los Angeles, San Francisco and New York will fall to $625,500, from the current level of $729,750.

The FHA doesn't make mortgages but instead insures lenders against defaults on loans that meet its standards. It allows borrowers to make down payments of just 3.5% and has played an outsized role backstopping mortgages in the aftermath of the housing bust.

Congress temporarily increased the FHA's maximum loan limits in 2008 as the downturn worsened. Before the increase, the FHA couldn't guarantee loans that exceeded $367,000.

Congress extended the more generous loan limits repeatedly, most recently in 2011. Those higher limits were set to expire at the end of this year. The most recent extension has allowed the FHA to guarantee loans in many parts of the country that were larger than those eligible for backing by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.

The FHA's loan limits vary by county and are calculated using certain formulas that are pegged to local median home values. Loan limits are as low as $271,050 in the nation's least expensive housing markets and as high as $625,500 in the most expensive ones. The FHA hadn't yet issued more details on where loan limits would fall for individual counties that are in between those boundaries.

Previously, the FHA could back mortgages up to 125% of the median home price for a given county. Beginning next year, the FHA will only back loans up to 115% of the area median price. That will take the loan limit for San Bernardino, Calif., for example, to $355,350 next year from $500,000 on a single-family home. Limits in Chicago will fall to $365,700 from $410,000.

The FHA has experienced heavy losses as a result of loans it guaranteed as the housing downturn deepened, and earlier this year it received a $1.7 billion infusion from the U.S. Treasury, the first in the agency's 79-year history. Officials have repeatedly boosted insurance fees charged to borrowers in a bid to replenish reserves. Fee increases have made private-mortgage insurance options more competitive, and the FHA has witnessed volumes decline this year.

Officials said Friday that the loan limit declines, which had been largely anticipated, would help the agency reduce its role in the market.

"As the housing market continues its recovery, it is important for FHA to evaluate the role we need to play," said FHA Commissioner Carol Galante. "Implementing lower loan limits is an important and appropriate step as private capital returns to portions of the market and enables FHA to concentrate on those borrowers that are still underserved."

The FHA limits are separate from those used by Fannie and Freddie, which have a national loan limit of $417,000 but will buy loans as large as $625,500 in more expensive housing markets. The regulator of the two mortgage-finance giants had said earlier this year that it might drop both the national limit and the high-cost limit, but said last week that it wouldn't proceed with those declines for now.

Central Bank Details Risks to Ireland's Banks; Economy Showing Signs of Recovery but Remains Vulnerable
Eamon Quinn
433 words
6 December 2013

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

DUBLINIreland's economy is showing signs of recovery but remains vulnerable to high levels of household and business debt, the central bank said Friday, in a report designed to identify the potential risks the country faces as it prepares to exit its international bailout.

Official data have shown that a strong increase in employment suggests that Ireland's so-far slow pace of economic recovery was on course to pick up next year, the central bank said. Other bright signs include a rise in Dublin house prices, an increase in commercial property transactions, and the fall in Irish government bond yields to their lowest levels since before the start of the country's property and banking crisis more than five years ago.

The central bank warns, however, that the country's loss-making banks remain vulnerable to high levels of debt held by both households and businesses, a legacy of the collapse of the property market that brought Ireland close to national bankruptcy and eventually forced it to embrace a bailout with the European Union and International Monetary Fund three years ago.

"As Ireland exits the official assistance program, the macro financial environment remains challenging," the central bank said in its latest Macro-Financial Review.

According to the report, "the level of distress among small and medium enterprise borrowers is particularly acute", meaning that the banks continue to face considerable risks that their business loans will turn sour.

Banks appear to have increased their levels of provisions, but "uncertainties remain about whether banks are sufficiently provisioned to cope with the outstanding stock of distressed loans," it said in the report.

Three of Ireland's largest banksBank of Ireland PLC, Allied Irish Banks PLC and Permanent TSBsaid Monday they wouldn't need more capital following so-called Balance Sheet Assessments by the central bank. Bank of Ireland said, however, it may need to increase its level of provisions across its loans books.

The central bank now publishes regular such reviews to consider the risks facing the economy. Ireland's regulators want to avoid the mistakes of the past when they failed to identify missteps by its banks that brought the country close to bankruptcy.

The government has announced Dec. 15 as the formal date when it exits its three-year bailout.

The report didn't provide new growth forecasts, but, following two years of effective economic stagnation, the bank reiterates government forecasts that the economy will grow by about 2% next year.

Ilona Billington

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LONDONU.K. Chancellor of the Exchequer George Osborne on Thursday said that nonresidents who own homes in the U.K. will from April 2015 be taxed on any profits made when they sell the property.

Property market analysts said that may dissuade some nonresident investors from buying homes in London, a development that could help ease the rapid rise in prices over recent months.

But while the move came amid concerns that a heating up of the housing market in London could ultimately turn sour, hurting banks and borrowers, the government said its principal concern was fairness.

With this tax change, treatment of nonresidents' home ownership will become more similar to that of residents who buy a second property for investment purposes.

The government said it would open consultations on the details of the new tax. Many tax experts expect that the charge will only apply to gains made after April 2015.

Under current rules, owners of residential property who don't live in the U.K. are exempt from the capital gains taxcurrently set at a top rate of 28%which U.K. residents must pay when they sell a second property. This makes U.K. property a popular choice for overseas investors looking for a safe place for their cash, and in part explains the soaring price of London homes.

"Today's raid on the pockets of overseas private landlords will align this area of the U.K.'s tax system with many other jurisdictions, including the U.S. and many of our European neighbors," said John Cooney, head of private client tax services for Ernst and Young.

However, Mr. Cooney said it would be important for the government to communicate to foreign investors that the rule change simply subjects them to the same treatment as U.K. residents, and isn't discriminatory. "It will be important for the U.K.'s competitiveness thatoverseas investors realize that this is a targeted measure and not the first stage of a cash grab by [Her Majesty's] Treasury," he said.

According to online estate agency Rightmove, the average asking price for a London home advertised on its website in November was 517,276 ($847,453), down slightly from the high of 544,232 in October, but still almost 7% higher than November 2012.

The U.K. average asking price in November was 246,237, according to Rightmove.

U.S. stocks slid Wednesday as investors weighed the outlook for the Federal Reserve to start paring stimulus efforts this month after a raft of economic reports.

"It's one of these periods where you never know whether good news is going to be good or bad for stocks, because of this overhang of central-bank involvement," said Colleen Supran, principal with Bingham, Osborn & Scarborough LLC, which manages $2.7 billion.

Stocks have slipped in recent days as a slate of better-than-expected economic data reports have been released, which supported expectations that the Fed could start trimming its easy-money policies, a process known as "tapering," as soon as this month.

"Investors have gotten used to the idea that at some point the Fed will need to start getting out of the way," said Ms. Supran. "But that is not going to be a straight and smooth road. We're certainly going to have volatility in stock prices."

As the S&P 500 enters the last month of the year with a 26% gain, investors say they are pausing to determine their outlook for 2014. Many say it has gotten more difficult to take a strong stance on investment ideas after the steep rise in U.S. and European markets.

"It's tough," said Alan Gayle, senior investment strategist at RidgeWorth Investments, which manages $49 billion in Atlanta. "There are lots of crosscurrents for investors and portfolio managers into the end of the year. Ultimately, we're looking for signs that the economy is maintaining momentum."

Wednesday's data had a mixed record against economists' forecasts, but generally indicated continued growth.

"They're consistent with the general trend that we've seen of moderate growth," said Dan Greenhaus, chief global strategist at BTIG.

Activity in the U.S. services sector expanded at a slower pace in November. The Institute for Supply Management's nonmanufacturing purchasing managers index fell by more than expected for that month to 53.9 from October's 55.4. It was forecast to slip to 55. Readings above 50 indicate expansion.

New home sales surged in October, rising to an annual rate of 444,000, on expectations of a rise to 426,000. The ADP report is seen as a preview of the U.S. government's November employment report, due Friday.

J.C. Penney declined 3.6% despite a report from the retailer showing same-store sales for November rose 10.1% above year-earlier levels.

OmniVision Technologies fell 6.3% after the mobile camera-sensor maker posted better-than-expected fiscal second-quarter earnings and revenue, but provided a downbeat outlook for the current quarter.

Markets trimmed early sharp losses, with the Stoxx Europe 600 index shedding 0.6% to 317.24, marking a fourth straight day of losses.

Shares of Sage Group rallied 7.3% after the software firm raised its dividend by 6% and said it is on track to meet its organic revenue-growth target in 2015.

Banks posted losses after European Union regulators fined a group of six financial institutions a record-breaking penalty of $2.32 billion for interest-rate fixing. Among fined banks, Deutsche Bank lost 1% and Societe Generale fell 1%.

Standard Chartered slid 6.5% in London after the bank said difficult market conditions were expected the rest of the year and that income for 2013 would be broadly flat on 2012.

Tesco fell 0.5% in London after the supermarkets chain said it is performing in line with market expectations, but that same-store sales declined in the U.K. in the third quarter.

Retail sales in the euro zone declined 0.2% in October, missing expectations of a 0.2% rise. Markit's composite purchasing managers' index fell to 51.7 in November from October's 51.9.

The U.S. economy expanded at a "modest to moderate" pace in recent months, the Federal Reserve said Wednesday in a report that showed mixed economic conditions across the nation just weeks before a key policy decision by the central bank.

The central bank's beige book, which assesses the economic environment in the Fed's 12 districts, cited strength in the U.S. manufacturing sector and consumer spending. Seven districts reported steady growth rates, while four districts indicated a less robust expansion than the others. One region simply said economic activity continued to expand.

The snapshot, based on data gathered from early October through Nov. 22, comes two weeks before the Fed's Dec. 17-18 policy meeting. The Fed will decide at that meeting whether to start pulling back its $85 billion-a-month bond-buying program, which is aimed at lowering borrowing costs to spur stronger spending, hiring and growth.

Uncertainty about the Fed's next steps remains a key risk hanging over investors and employers. Fed officials expect to start scaling back the program "in coming months," if the overall economy and labor market continue to improve, according to minutes from its last policy meeting in October.

A separate survey released Wednesday by the Business Roundtable, a group of top corporate executives, found CEOs the most bullish they've been about the economy in almost two years. The survey's economic-outlook index for the fourth quarter rose to its highest point since early 2012. Executives said clearer signals from Washington could help the economy accelerate.

"We have an economy that is on the cusp of growing at more than the 2-to-3% we've seen," said Boeing Co. CEO Jim McNerney, chairman of the group. "Washington sorting themselves out would give businesses a more predictable environment to invest the cash on their balance sheets and hire people."

Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke will meet the CEOs at the Business Roundtable's quarterly meeting Thursday in Washington, as one of several speakers to talk with the executives. The event is closed to the public, and the roster of CEOs who plan to attend hasn't been released. The group has about 200 member CEOs from large companies, whose planning decisions could be influenced by the direction of interest rates and whose plans could influence the Fed's policy decisions.

Sales had slowed sharply during the summer partly due to spiking mortgage rates tied to the Fed's signals. New-home sales rose 25% in October from the prior month to an annual rate of 444,000. Tourism increased in most districts, although the 16-day partial federal government shutdown in October "had a negative impact in some areas," according to the report.

The residential housing market showed signs of strength in apartment construction, but there was some slowing in single-family home sales due to seasonal factors. Still, sales remain largely above year-ago levels, the report said.

Banking conditions were largely stable, with some improvements in loan demand, the report said. Several districts reported an easing of lending standards

Overall price pressures remained largely contained. But businesses in many districts voiced concern about future cost increases due to the Affordable Care Act and other types of federal regulation.

U.S. New-Home Sales Surged in October; Sharp Drop in Supply Could Push Builders Into Action
Josh Mitchell
590 words
4 December 2013
