Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary
1020 words
30 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
28
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Taping Up India's Broken House Quickly

Easy money let emerging markets take it too easy in recent years. Now some are trying to patch things up quickly, before global liquidity starts to tighten.

The U.S. Federal Reserve scared markets over the summer, when it suggested it would scale back bond purchases. Though the Fed isn't changing its bond-buying course for now, emerging-market policy makers who grew addicted to cheap foreign money don't want to take chances. Last week, Malaysia and Indonesia promised to trim subsidies and shore up public finances.

Now India's hard-pressed central bank is doing its part.

India was among the emerging economies hardest hit by the Fed's suggestion it would scale back. The country's current-account deficit widened to an unhealthy 4.9% of gross domestic product in the quarter ended in June, meaning it needed more foreign capital to finance the economy. Yet what foreigners got in exchange for investing in India was negative real interest rates and falling growth. With a hint U.S. rates might be more attractive than zero, foreigners turned tail, sending the rupee plunging.

The Reserve Bank of India's new chief, Raghuram Rajan, raised the benchmark interest rate by a quarter percentage point Tuesday, after an identical increase last month.

The increases restore confidence and show that Mr. Rajan is serious about taming consumer prices, which have persistently risen at an annual rate exceeding 8% for the past five years.

The RBI previously didn't respond strongly enough: In inflation-adjusted terms, short-term borrowing costs stayed below zero for most of the past five years and sent India's savings rate down to a five-year low.

The telegenic Mr. Rajan has gotten markets comfortable with India, for now. The Bombay Stock Exchange's benchmark Sensex index climbed 1.7% Tuesday, bucking equities' typical response to rate increases. The Sensex is near its all-time high, while the rupee is up 10% since Mr. Rajan came on board.

It is important that investors not get ahead of themselves. Real change requires that New Delhi enact tough land and tax reforms, slash fuel subsidies and get spending under control.

The rally in India's stocks and currency also owes itself to short-term boosts. The Fed's decision to delay tapering off the bond buying is one. Another, as Mr. Rajan noted in the policy address, is the central bank's provision of dollars to India's oil importers, presumably by dipping into its foreign-exchange reserves.

India and other emerging markets are taking advantage of a reprieve to get their houses in order. If they keep this up, they might survive the next taper storm better.

-- Abheek Bhattacharya

Fed Is House Bound Amid Weak Pending Home Sales

The U.S. housing market is probably not in nearly as much trouble as Monday's dismal report on pending home sales suggested. But the Federal Reserve won't want to take any chances.

The National Association of Realtors said its index of pending home sales, which measures contract signings for previously owned homes, fell 5.6% in September from August. The drop put the index 1.2% below its September 2012 level, marking the first year-over-year decline since May 2011.

Clearly, the rise in mortgage rates -- to 4.3% at the end of September from a low of 3.35% this spring -- that came amid expectations the Fed would reduce its bond purchases at its meeting last month did some damage.

But at least some of the drop in pending home sales was probably payback for a flurry of contract signings that began in May, when the pending home-sales index jumped 5.8% from a month earlier. One big reason for the stepped up activity: Worries about rising rates prompted many potential buyers to get off the fence.

Indeed, by July, 10% of respondents to the University of Michigan's monthly survey of consumers who said it was a good time to buy a home cited borrowing ahead of higher interest rates as a reason. That was the largest share since 2006.

Moreover, as far as actual housing activity goes, September's big drop is less significant than meets the eye. September is a generally weak month for contract signings, while the spring and early summer are more active as prospective buyers look to move ahead of the new school year.

The Realtors group adjusts the pending home-sales index for such swings, amplifying seasonally weak months and damping strong ones. Put otherwise, housing activity was even stronger in the spring and summer than the index suggests. The payback, in terms of actual lost pending sales, wasn't as severe.

All the same, it will be a while before it is clear September was more payback than the start of a real problem. Add it to the long list of reasons for the Fed to let more time pass before it puts tapering back on the table.

-- Justin Lahart

Overheard

Is the Bank of England's guidance on interest rates, with a 7% unemployment threshold and three exceptions to it known as knockout clauses -- two based on the path of future inflation and one on whether monetary policy poses a risk to financial stability -- too complicated? No, says Spencer Dale, BOE chief economist and a member of the Monetary Policy Committee. Indeed, at an event Monday, he was able to show proudly that the essential tenets of forward guidance fit comfortably on a single slide.

Alas, he proceeded to explain that, under the forward-guidance policy, investors should pay particular attention to every speech from every one of the committee's nine members. Mr. Dale went on to say that if a member were to judge one of the inflation knockouts breached, he or she might vote to raise rates, or not, or simply declare themselves no longer bound by guidance. In that case, investors might need a spreadsheet to keep track.

U.S. News: Jump in Housing Costs Hits Pending Sales
By Sarah Portlock
497 words
29 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- Higher mortgage rates and rising home prices scared off prospective home buyers in September, sparking the fourth straight monthly drop in a key measure of home sales.

The National Association of Realtors' gauge of pending sales of existing homes fell 5.6% last month from August, the trade group said Monday. That pushed the measure down to its lowest level since December 2012. It fell 1.2% year-to-year, marking the first time in more than two years that pending-home-sales activity was lower than a year earlier.

The Realtors group attributed the drop to the rising costs of purchasing a home, as well as budget battles in Congress that hit consumers' confidence.

Economists surveyed by Dow Jones had expected a 0.5% monthly rise. "This is worse than we expected and it surely lays to rest the notion that the housing market is strong enough to absorb the rise in mortgage rates with no ill-effects," said Ian Shepherdson, chief economist at Pantheon Macroeconomics.

Pending home sales are considered a timely gauge of the housing market because the report measures when a contract is signed but the transaction hasn't closed. The closing can be one to two months later.

The number of contracts signed has been declining steadily since May. During that month, the sales figure shot up nearly 6% from the previous month as many fence-sitters rushed to buy homes and lock in lower mortgage rates just as rates started to rise.

Between April and August, the average rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage rose by more than a full percentage point, according to Freddie Mac. It peaked at 4.58% in August, and hovered around those levels for much of September.

Home prices have also been rising as more would-be buyers submit bids for homes. Economists expect the S&P/Case-Shiller home-price index for August, set for release Tuesday, will show a 12.5% gain in prices in August from a year earlier in 20 of the largest U.S. cities. All 20 cities have shown year-over-year gains for seven straight months. The Realtors group said last week that its measure of median home prices for September rose 11.7% from a year earlier, continuing a path of increases but at a slower pace than in recent months.

In other economic news:

The nation's industrial output returned to prerecession levels for the first time last month, though weak gains in manufacturing highlighted the sector's slow recovery. Industrial productiona measure of output at U.S. factories, utilities and mines -- increased a seasonally adjusted 0.6% in September from the prior month, the Federal Reserve said Monday.

The gain, driven largely by rebounding utility use, pushed overall output back to its 2007 average for the first time since the recession.

US
Home Prices Rose in August, but Pace of Gains Is Slowing
Ruth Mantell
425 words
29 October 2013
09:32
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTONEven as annual home-price growth in August hit the fastest pace in more than seven years, data released Tuesday morning show that monthly gains are slowing down.

Home prices in August were up 12.8% from the year-earlier period, the fastest pace since early 2006, according to the Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller home-price index. Of the 20 cities in the main index, 13 posted double-digit annual gains.

Pent-up demand and limited inventory have been supporting home prices. Some of that pent-up demand was unleashed in August when buyers rushed to lock in low mortgage rates and home sales spiked.

Cities that were hit particularly hard when the bubble burst have been posting outsized annual growth. In August, Las Vegas home prices were up 29% from the year-earlier period. Yet prices there remain 47% below the peak level. Meanwhile, prices are also jumping in areas with lots of competition for homes. In San Francisco, for example, home prices in August rose 25% from the year-earlier period.

However, monthly details of the Case-Shiller report show some softening. Home prices rose 1.3% in August, the smallest monthly gain since March, as 16 of the 20 cities saw slower growth. After seasonal adjustments, home prices in August rose 0.9%, below a recent peak of 1.9% in March.

"Recent increases in mortgage rates and fewer mortgage applications are two factors in these shifts," said David Blitzer, index committee chairman at S&PDow Jones Indices.

Looking forward, economists expect home-price growth to moderate, as rising mortgage rates curb some demand, cutting pressure on prices.

"With existing home sales now clearly slowing quite sharply, we have to expect price gains to slow a good deal further over the next few months, but we don't expect to see any outright declines unless the inventory of homes for sale rises well above its current level," said Ian Shepherdson, chief economist at Pantheon Macroeconomics.

Price growth could also slow due to expanding inventories as more sellers become willing and able to place their homes on the market. The partial government shutdown that delayed some loan processing and shook consumer confidence could also slow price growth.

Despite the blistering pace of annual growth, home prices in August were about 20% below a 2006 peak.

US
Wholesale Prices Tame as Fed Meets
By Jonathan House
805 words
29 October 2013
12:43
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTONU.S. wholesale prices fell in September, a new sign that inflation remains in check as the Federal Reserve considers its easy-money policies in a two-day meeting set to conclude Wednesday

The producer-price index[http://www.bls.gov.proxy.library.brocku.ca/ppi], which measures how much companies pay for everything from footwear to computers, fell 0.1% in September from August, the Labor Department said Tuesday. The decline was primarily the result of an 18% decline in fresh-vegetable prices.

Excluding food and energy components, core wholesale prices rose 0.1%. Compared to the same period last year, September wholesale prices rose by 0.3%, the lowest annual level since October 2009.

Wholesale prices have been subdued since mid-2012, the result of slack demand in the U.S. and abroad. Weak global economies have helped put a damper on energy prices. U.S. agricultural prices have been soft after a mid-2012 spike caused by a drought last year.

"Inflation is taking a very, very long nap," said Michael Montgomery, an economist at IHS Global Insight. "Almost nothing is causing significant upward pressure on prices," he said.

The latest inflation reading comes as Fed officials gather Tuesday and Wednesday for their first policy meeting since deciding last month to leave unchanged their $85 billion-a-month bond-buying program to stimulate the economy.

The Fed is expected to leave its program unchanged Wednesday. Many economists now expect the central bank to maintain its current pace of bond purchases at least until late this year or early next year, due to weaker labor-market performance and volatile economic reports from the government shutdown.

Continued weak inflation will likely reinforce those expectations. On Wednesday, the Labor Department will publish September data on consumer prices.

Low inflation gives the Fed more scope to pursue its easy-money policies. Continued efforts by the central bank to hold down interest rates could be good news for an economic recovery that remains stuck in low gear. Data from the National Association of Realtors on Monday showed a sharp decline in September pending home sales, a new sign that higher mortgage rates and rising sale prices have dented the housing rebound.

The sharp decline in September fresh-vegetable prices came after they surged 27% in August. This category is highly volatile, conditioned by weather and crop conditions from month to month.

Energy wholesale prices rose by 0.5% in September. Natural-gas and heating-oil prices rose, while gasoline fell slightly.

Prices for intermediate goodsarticles like sugar and leather that require further processingrose a modest 0.1% in September. Prices of raw materials, known as crude goods, rose by 0.5%. J.P. Morgan economist Jimmy Coonan said that low price growth throughout the production chain signals that "inflation pressures should remain benign."

Separately, the Labor Department is revamping its system for measuring wholesale prices, the latest overhaul by a U.S. statistical agency to capture a broader range of economic activity.

The government's current producer-price index had become outmoded as the U.S. economy increasingly shifted from manufacturing toward services.

The new measurement system aims to capture for the first time the prices companies pay for services and for construction. It will also measure for the first time the prices companies pay for goods and services that are destined for export or for government customers.

The Labor Department's Bureau of Labor Statistics estimates the new system will allow it to measure wholesale prices throughout 75% of the U.S. economy, up from 33% currently. It will also bring the government's producer price index closer in line with its consumer price index, which already measures the prices of many services.

The new index will replace the old one when the Labor Department publishes its report on January 2014 producer prices in February. Until then, it will continue publishing wholesale price data according to the old methodology, as well as data calculated according to the new methodology with a two-week delay.

For August, the last month for which both sets of data are available, the official release showed wholesale prices rose 1.4% on a nonseasonally adjusted basis over the prior 12 months, while the data prepared according to the new methodology showed a 1.9% increase.

Earlier this year, the Commerce Department's Bureau of Economic Analysis took its own steps to expand the scope of its coverage by adding new measures and data for its report on gross domestic product. One of the biggest changes is how the agency now measures what it calls the "knowledge economy" through investments in research and development and entertainment and the arts.

SHANGHAIShanghai-based developer Greenland Holdings Group plans to invest 1 billion Australian dollars (US$957 million) in real estate in Sydney and Melbourne in the next two years, as part of an aggressive global expansion [http://blogs.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/deals/2013/03/19/chinas-greenland-group-plans-property-investments-in-u-s-australia/] that has already included billion-dollar projects in the U.S. this year.

But Zhang Yuliang, chairman of the state-owned conglomerate, said the plan depends on whether the welcome mat remains out to investment as well as immigrants and foreign students.

"How much we invest in Australia depends on the country's macroeconomic policies, how open it would be to foreign investment and trade," he told reporters Tuesday.

"If Australia closes its market, it would be detrimental to the economy," said Mr. Zhang.

In March Greenland spent A$480 million on a residential and hotel project in Sydney called Sydney Greenland Center. It also has an interest in a project near a horse-racing track in Melbourne.

But there has been some backlash in Australia, with Chinese buyers blamed for pushing property prices out of reach of local residents.

The Australian government is eager to strengthen business ties with China but faces calls from within the ruling coalition to more wary of foreign investment in industries such as agriculture. Prime Minister Tony Abbott, as a candidate campaigning ahead of September's federal election, said his Liberal-National coalition would increase regulatory scrutiny of sales of agricultural land and businesses to overseas buyers.

However, he also has said he has plans to secure a free-trade agreement with China, and he's proposed a low-tax zone in the remote Northern Territory to help attract investment and encourage farming.

Australia is home to a large number of international students and mainland Chinese [http://online.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/news/articles/SB10001424127887323466204578382673621513536], which helps buoy demand for Greenland's properties, Mr. Zhang said.

"For our Australia projects, perhaps 50% to 60% would be sold to the local Chinese population and foreign students," he said, adding that Greenland expects to sell half its Sydney project within a month of its launch in late November.

Greenland is also keen on casino projects in Australia, Mr. Zhang said, adding that it is already developing a casino in South Korea.

Greenland has spent billions of dollars this year alone acquiring large projects abroad, including a 70% stake in a 15-tower apartment project in Brooklyn, New York [http://online.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/news/articles/SB10001424052702304520704579127822887005590]. Greenland estimated the whole project with cost more than $5 billion.

Greenland is among the highest-profile mainland property companies to go on a shopping spree this year, but it is hardly alone: Rival China Vanke has projects in San Francisco, Hong Kong and Singapore, while Dalian Wanda Group is splashing out on a $1 billion luxury hotel in London.

"Internationalization is a must for us, not just in the U.S., but also Europe, Australia and Asia," said Mr. Zhang, who defended the role of Chinese investors.

"Chinese buyers are owner-occupiers and their purchases will benefit Australia's employment numbers and tax receipts," said Mr. Zhang. "It's prejudice to link Chinese buyers to speculation."

Greenland's real-estate acquisitions abroad have been closely watched by distressed-property developers and foreign governments. The company notes that it is focusing on mature cities with a sizable Chinese population that also attracts Chinese tourists.

In recent weeks, Mr. Zhang said Greenland has met with Australian Trade Minister Andrew Robb and London Mayor Boris Johnson.

"Mr. Robb suggested more open policies, which is welcome," said Mr. Zhang. Australian trade officials weren't available late Tuesday their time for comment.

Commenting on the company's recent investments in the U.S.in addition to its tentative Brooklyn deal, in July Greenland bought a US$1 billion mixed-used project in Los AngelesMr. Zhang said that he didn't see U.S. projects as high-risk. Greenland will focus on opportunities in New York, Los Angeles and San Francisco, Mr. Zhang said.

"The economic recovery in the U.S. is relatively good and asset prices are reasonable," he said.

Despite its global push, Greenland remains positive about the domestic property market overall, according to the Greenland chief.

While bigger cities such as Shanghai and Beijing are having a hard time keeping prices in check, and need government measures to help, in second- and third-tier cities prices are rising only moderately.

"There is no need for the central government to implement national curbsrather, the local governments should carry out their own property policies," he added. "I still have confidence in China's housing market for the next five to 10 years."

Market Report: Europe Stocks Slip; Record for S&P 500
By Tommy Stubbington and Michele Maatouk

The Wall Street Journal Europe

English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

European stocks drifted lower in quiet trading as disruptions following a storm that hit the U.K. overnight helped keep trading volumes low. Investors remained wary of making bold moves ahead of U.S. economic news expected this week.

The benchmark Stoxx Europe 600 index slipped 0.2% to 319.49. Germany's DAX edged down 0.1% to 8978.65 and France's CAC 40 fell 0.5% to 4251.61. But London's FTSE 100 edged up 0.1% to 6725.82.

In the U.S., stocks closed little changed, but the S&P 500 index reached another record high as investors took disappointing results from blue-chip drug maker Merck and mixed economic data in stride.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average eased 1.35 points, or less than 0.1%, to 15568.93. The Dow erased gains of as much as 28.81 points in the final hour of trading. The S&P 500 index gained 2.34 points, or 0.1%, to 1762.11, while the Nasdaq Composite gave up 3.23 points, or 0.1%, to 3940.13.

The S&P 500 closed at a record high for a second straight session, and the sixth time in eight sessions. The Dow ended 0.7% shy of its Sept. 18 all-time high close of 15676.94.

"The path of least resistance is higher," said Randy Billhardt, head of capital markets at investment bank MLV & Co. "The bad news is shrugged off, and the good news is being embraced."

Dow component Merck dropped 2.6% after it reported adjusted third-quarter earnings above analysts' estimates but revenue that fell a bit shy. Sales of Januvia, a diabetes drug, declined.

Still, "earnings for the most part have been well received," Mr. Billhardt said. "The market as a whole has held onto some pretty significant gains for the year, which in itself is a pretty significant achievement."

With about half the S&P 500 companies having reported results through Friday, 75% have beat earnings estimates while 52% have topped revenue forecasts, according to FactSet. The year-over-year earnings growth rate for the third quarter is 2.3%, FactSet said.

Trading in Europe was quiet. Some traders and investors were away from their desks because many of their children's schools were on holiday. Volumes were further dented by the storm that hit the U.K. overnight, causing travel cancellations and delays for commuters and keeping some traders at home.

A lot of European trading is conducted from London.

U.S. industrial production for September rose 0.6% from August, exceeding expectations of 0.4% growth. Meanwhile, pending home sales for September fell 5.6% to the lowest levels seen since December, missing expectations of a 0.5% increase.

Investors were awaiting data on U.S. retail sales and inflation due Tuesday, the ADP employment report on Wednesday, and manufacturing figures Friday.

London-listed Aggreko, the world's largest temporary-power supplier, surged 6% after the company said it expects full-year pretax profit to be in line with the market's expectations.

Swedish lock maker Assa Abloy gained 3.8% after announcing plans to launch a restructuring program in the fourth quarter, as it reported slightly higher-than-expected third-quarter revenue.

The euro was fetching $1.3786 in late New York trading from $1.3807 late Friday.

Crude for December delivery gained 83 cents a barrel, or 0.85%, to $98.68 on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold for October delivery slipped 40 cents a troy ounce to $1,352.00 on the Comex division of Nymex.

On Friday, the S&P 500 rose 0.4% to close at a record for a fifth time in seven sessions, while the Nasdaq Composite closed at a 13-year high.

Dow component Merck dropped 2.6% after it reported adjusted third-quarter earnings that topped analysts' estimates but revenue that fell a bit shy. Sales of Januvia, a diabetes drug, declined.

Apple edged 0.5% higher. The technology company is slated to report fiscal fourth-quarter results after the close. The stock has gained 6% in the two weeks leading up to the results.

"Earnings for the most part have been well-received," said Randy Billhardt, head of capital markets at investment bank MLV & Co. "The market as a whole has held onto some pretty significant gains for the year, which in itself is a pretty significant achievement."

The S&P 500 has gained more than 6% since the unofficial kickoff of earnings-reporting season after the Oct. 8 close and is now up 23% this year.

CF Industries Holdings advanced 4.2% after the company agreed to sell its phosphate business to fellow fertilizer company Mosaic for $1.4 billion. Mosaic gained 0.2%.

Biogen Idec rose 1.3% after the biotechnology company reported better-than-expected third-quarter adjusted earnings and revenue, helped by strong sales of its multiple sclerosis treatments, and lifted its full-year outlook.

Industrial production for September rose 0.6% on the month, exceeding expectations of 0.4% growth. Capacity utilization rose to 78.3%, the highest rate since July 2008 and above forecasts of 78%.

Meanwhile, pending home sales for September fell 5.6% to the lowest levels seen since December and missing expectations of a 0.5% increase.

European Stocks

Shares retreated after a string of three weekly gains, hurt by auto makers posting big losses after a broker downgrade.

The Stoxx Europe 600 index dropped 0.2%, to 319.49.

J.P. Morgan Cazenove cut the auto sector to "neutral" from "overweight" and instead moved funds to utilities, raising them to "neutral" from "underweight."

Car makers Peugeot skidded 5.8% and Renault fell 3.7% in Paris, Fiat dropped 3.2% in Milan and BMW and Daimler both lost 1.5% in Frankfurt.

Randgold Resources gained 4.6% after the gold miner said it plans to "aggressively" explore opportunities in the Ivory Coast.

Tullow Oil fell 2.2% after the oil explorer said it has temporarily suspended all operation at two areas in Kenya due to labor unrest.

Gemalto lost 1.5% after Credit Suisse cut the digital-security firm to "neutral" from "outperform" and lowed revenue estimates.

Shifting Tides: Sandy's Legacy: Higher Home Prices
By Josh Dawsey
2069 words
28 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

ORTLEY BEACH, N.J. -- John Anello had visited the Jersey Shore since he was a small child and always wanted a beach home there.

Superstorm Sandy has granted his wish -- and made him part of a national demographic shift that has driven up real-estate prices, while sowing social discord, in some coastal communities.

After Sandy, Mr. Anello says he paid $296,000 for a lot in Ortley Beach, where a small home was destroyed by the storm. He plans to build a 2,500-square-foot house on 8-foot pilings for about $350,000.

"It's a time to buy," says the 52-year-old Mr. Anello, an automotive technician in West Orange, N.J., with his own business. "I guarantee that you'll see a lot of people moving in and rebuilding the whole area. My house will be worth double what it is worth now in five years."

A year after Sandy swept the coast, inflicting what New Jersey estimates at more than $38 billion in damage to the state, some shore towns are still grappling with closed streets, broken streetlights and unfinished boardwalks.

The state, known for its summer tradition of visitors streaming to its beaches, has hundreds of thousands of homes -- including at least 120,000 seasonal homes -- in areas slammed by Sandy, according to the U.S. Census Bureau.

In more-modest communities, Sandy has accelerated a trend that real-estate experts see in many waterfront communities elsewhere in the U.S.: The rising cost of housing, insurance and taxes has already turned many vacation spots into havens affordable mainly to the more affluent.

After a storm's damage, many less-wealthy longtime owners find the only financially viable option is to sell. That moves many such properties further up the economic ladder, as buyers like Mr. Anello build bigger, more luxurious homes where bungalows once stood.

The result has been a surprise in these coastal communities: Many have found that storms were ultimately followed by real-estate prices that rose, rather than fell as feared.

After Hurricane Andrew hit Florida in 1992, a Federal Housing Finance Agency study showed values dramatically increased for the hardest-hit areas before eventually settling back to pre-storm rates of increase.

In 1988, a year before Hurricane Hugo battered South Carolina, typical oceanfront lots went for $200,000 on Isle of Palms, a vacation destination, says Michael Scarafile, president of Carolina One Real Estate in Charleston. After the storm, much of the rebuilding was of bigger, more luxurious houses, he says. By 2006, he says, his average listing on the island was $2 million, partially accelerated by the storm.

"Overall, it was very healthy for the market," he says. "A lot of the bungalows that were built 50 years ago went away."

On the flip side of the trend are people like Jim Torsiello. He bought a vacation bungalow in Ortley Beach for $120,000 in 1986 and shared it over the decades with family and friends who crowded in beds and slept on the floor to be near the shore. He hoped the summer home would stay in the family.

But Sandy sent 32 inches of water into his 900-square-foot, three-bedroom home, forcing him to tear out the walls. Mr. Torsiello, 70, says it would cost about $80,000 to repair and he is leaning toward selling it.

"I am not a millionaire," says the retired flower-shop owner. "I don't have the money to fix it."

That phenomenon is showing signs of changing the social fabric along New Jersey's beaches. Many owners of shore homes there, some built 70 years ago and handed down over generations, are finding the costs of post-Sandy repair out of reach.

Many, like Mr. Torsiello, didn't have flood insurance, and their homeowners policies didn't cover much of the storm damage -- he says he received $1,800 from his policy. Federal and state disaster relief generally doesn't cover second homes such as his.

A heavily damaged house in Mr. Torsiello's neighborhood -- several blocks from the beach -- might sell for the value of its land, roughly $165,000 to $185,000 for a typical lot, then be torn down to make way for a new house that might cost $400,000, says Tim O'Shea, office manager at Birchler Realtors, an Ortley Beach real-estate firm.

Mr. O'Shea says he recently sold two aging bungalows nearer the beach for $289,000 and $265,000. The buyers plan to demolish them to build more luxurious houses, he says. Of the 40 houses he has sold this year, most will be demolished. "It doesn't matter what the house looks like when we sell it," he says. "The people buying will knock it down and build as big a house as the town will let them."

When new buyers rebuild, they must meet stricter codes and must often do as Mr. Anello plans: build up off the ground. Mr. Anello says he doesn't fear storms because his home's newer building methods should withstand nature's ravages. "Everyone is going to build high," he says. "I can't ever see water levels going above eight feet."

As larger homes go up, real-estate officials say, some towns could benefit from the added tax revenue and the spending of more-affluent beachgoers. "Financially, 10 years from now, our town will be in far better shape than it was today," says Stephen Acropolis, the mayor of nearby Brick Township. "You're going to have these McMansions coming up everywhere."

But some fear that because there will be fewer homes with fewer residents, small businesses will suffer. "If you don't have these secondary residents back," says Paul Jeffrey, president of the nonprofit Ortley Beach Voters and Taxpayers Association, "the pizza shops and the hardware stores that depend on them don't make it."

Even before the storm, prices on beachfront properties were on the rise in New Jersey. Prices held up better on beaches during the financial crisis than elsewhere in the state, says Peter Reinhart, director of the Kislak Real Estate Institute at Monmouth University.

That has put shore homes out of reach for many would-be newcomers, in extreme cases, dramatically: The average listing in Mantoloking is about $2.2 million, according to Trulia, an online real-estate service, and about $1.2 million in Bay Head, as of mid-October.

Less-affluent longtime owners have nonetheless remained a big part of the social and business fabric, spending vacations in bungalows and smaller ranch houses that were long a cornerstone of the shore.

Built decades ago and passed through generations, these dwellings largely exist in middle-class communities like Ortley Beach, Brigantine, Brick Township and Long Beach Township. They are relatively small -- often less than 1,000 square feet -- but boast colorful beach signs, small porches and decades of memories.

Diane-Monica Lafortune called her duplex "Disgraceland," for reasons she says only a boisterous Irish family can understand. She bought it in 2000 for $295,000, she says. After her husband's death three months later, it became a sanctuary where she could try to forget her loss.

Every summer, Ms. Lafortune drove from her Montreal home to see family in New Jersey, staying in the duplex she shared with a sister. One year, she invited 148 family members for a reunion, where she says Irish priests in the family celebrated Mass and bottles of whiskey were consumed.

"After five days, we just had to kick everyone out," she says.

Her sister now wants out of the Ortley Beach duplex, which sustained major water damage during Sandy. They didn't have flood insurance. To rebuild, Ms. Lafortune, 58, says she would have to buy her sister's half, demolish the place, and rebuild to code -- at a cost of $500,000 or more. She says she is likely to sell.

Residents, real-estate agents and architects say several obstacles stand in the way of rebuilding. Older homes stand low to the ground and are often made of wood, which made them easy targets for Sandy's wind, rain and flooding.

Repairs can cost more than many people paid for homes, and many homeowners deemed flood insurance too costly even before the storm drove rates higher. In some of New Jersey's most-heavily affected areas, less than 50% had flood insurance, according to a study by the University of Pennsylvania's Wharton School and Resources for the Future, an independent think tank.

Some that had flood insurance are fighting with insurers and say they don't have savings to rebuild without winning the cases. The town of Toms River, which Ortley Beach is part of, estimates that about 70% of Ortley Beach's residences are second homes -- not eligible for government assistance.

The Federal Emergency Management Agency says 261,884 New Jersey households applied for FEMA grants; 61,375 were approved, with an average payment of $6,000, and others are being processed. FEMA doesn't give aid to second-home owners, a FEMA spokesman says, because "if you have a second home, you have something to go back to."

New Jersey says it has given about 15,000 people $10,000 grants to resettle after Sandy and another 140 people rebuilding-and-repair grants totaling $12 million, with another 200 grant signings scheduled and more being processed.

Michael Drewniak, a spokesman for Gov. Chris Christie, says Mr. Christie believes the federal government should give rebuilding aid to second-home owners. Without help, many of them are likely to be priced out, unable to pay for rebuilding or insurance, he says.

"There is a concern from immediately after the storm that all our shore neighborhoods won't be able to return to themselves as far as character and being populated by all income levels," he says. "The Jersey Shore was never just a place for large second homes owned by the well-to-do."

If homeowners don't elevate their houses out of harm's way -- in many cases by five feet or more -- they are likely to face flood-insurance premiums that could double or more under a new federal law that attempts to make homeowners pay the actual cost of living near the water. New FEMA flood maps are set to put more people into higher insurance categories if their homes aren't elevated.

Increased regulatory and insurance costs will likely raise the cost of owning in the area, says Jed Kolko, chief economist at Trulia. "It could put ownership further out of reach for lower and middle income people," he says. "It's quite possible the mix of people who own homes on the shore changes."

June Squillaro, 72, fears she may be one leaving that mix. Acres of dirt mounds sit in the neighborhood where her place was. She started visiting her family's home there more than 50 years ago with her parents. She consumed her first can of beer there. She later bought the place, and her children and grandchildren came to love it. Because she stayed all year, she kept keys for neighbors who lovingly called her "Aunt June."

Ms. Squillaro says her 800-square-foot bungalow by the ocean "was exactly where she wanted to be." She and her husband sold their primary house 10 years ago and chose to retire there.

The bungalow was devastated by Sandy and eventually razed. Insurance paid $122,000, she says, but she's not sure that will be enough to rebuild. They are renting a small condo -- FEMA granted them $4,000 in rental assistance -- and weighing whether they can afford to return. "I would do anything short of robbing a bank to get back," she says.

Mr. Anello, who bought the post-Sandy lot, says he looks forward to building for a long future in Ortley Beach. He plans to take his 13-year-old-daughter to the shore every weekend, and to retire there.

"I told her, 'Daddy has a chance to build your future,'" he says. "All these people had futures down there for generations. Those houses had been there for 50, 60, 70 years. Now I'm building my future down there."

Markets
Stocks Finish Little Changed; S&P 500 Sets Another High; Merck Sinks
By Tomi Kilgore
757 words
28 October 2013
16:35
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Stocks closed little changed, with the S&P 500 index reaching another record high, as investors took in stride disappointing results from blue-chip drug maker Merck and mixed economic data.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average slipped 1.35 points, or less than 0.1%, to 15568.93. The Dow erased gains of as much as 28.81 points in the final hour of trading.

The S&P 500 index gained 2.34 points, or 0.1%, to 1762.11, while the Nasdaq Composite gave up 3.23 points, or 0.1%, to 3940.13.

The S&P 500 closed at a record high for a second session in a row, and for the sixth time in eight sessions. The Dow ended 0.7% below its Sept. 18 all-time high close of 15676.94.

"The path of least resistance is higher," said Randy Billhardt, head of capital markets at investment bank MLV & Co. "The bad news is shrugged off, and the good news is being embraced."

Dow component Merck dropped after it reported adjusted third-quarter earnings above analysts' estimates, but revenue that fell a bit shy. Sales of Januvia, a diabetes drug, declined.

"Earnings for the most part have been well received," Mr. Billhardt said. "The market as a whole has held onto some pretty significant gains for the year, which in itself is a pretty significant achievement."

The S&P 500 has gained more than 6% since the unofficial kickoff of earnings reporting season after the Oct. 8 close, and is now up 24% so far this year.

With a about half the S&P 500 companies reporting results through Friday, 75% have beat earnings estimates while 52% have topped revenue forecasts, according to FactSet. The year-over-year earnings growth rate for the third quarter is 2.3%, FactSet said.

"It's all about corporate earnings right now. Earnings are looking strong," said Doug Cote, chief market strategist at ING U.S. Investment Management. "U.S. corporations have proved that despite all the uncertainties ... they can still put up record profits."

Industrial production for September rose 0.6% on the month, exceeding expectations of 0.4% growth. Capacity utilization rose to 78.3%, the highest rate since July 2008 and above forecasts of 78%.

Meanwhile, pending home sales for September fell 5.6% to the lowest levels seen since December, and missing expectations of a 0.5% increase.

The yield on the 10-year Treasury ticked up to 2.514%, from 2.503% late Friday.

Investors will also be looking ahead to a statement from the Federal Reserve on Wednesday, following the conclusion of a two-day meeting of the central bank's policy-setting committee. The Fed is widely expected to keep stimulus measures, including the $85 billion-a-month bond-purchasing program, intact.

Mr. Cote said although the Fed's accommodative policy has helped support the market, he believes the overall trend higher will continue to be driven by improving fundamentals. "We're in a bull market, based on sound fundamentals," Mr. Cote said. "As long as the earnings come in as they have been, you want to buy into volatility."

December crude-oil futures rose 0.8% to settle at $98.68 a barrel, erasing early losses, while October gold futures ticked down less than 0.1% to $1,352.00 an ounce.

Currency markets were largely quiet, with the dollar inching higher against the euro and the yen.

European markets closed mostly lower. The Stoxx Europe 600 slipped 0.2% after being up as much as 0.3% earlier in the session. Germany's DAX 30 index fell 0.1%, France's CAC 40 index lost 0.5% and the U.K.'s FTSE 100 index inched up 0.1%.

Asian markets moved broadly higher, rebounding from recent weakness. Japan's Nikkei Stock Average jumped 2.2%, China's Shanghai Composite added less than 0.1%, and Australia's S&P ASX 200 climbed 1% to a five-year high.

In other corporate news, J.C. Penney rallied following reports that the chief executive of the troubled retailer affirmed its outlook for fiscal third-quarter same-store sales growth.

Consol Energy slipped after the company agreed to sell five Appalachian coal mines to privately held Murray Energy in a deal valued at $3.5 billion.

New York
Real-Estate Prices Begin to Firm Up in Areas Hit by Flooding; Prices and Numbers of Home Sales in Flood-Prone Areas Begin to Stabilize
Josh Barbanel
866 words
28 October 2013
18:44
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Almost a year after superstorm Sandy, a study shows the prices and number of sales for homes in New York City's most flood-prone neighborhoods have stabilized and even begun to strengthen.

While the Manhattan condo market had proved resilient even after Sandy flooded basements and lobbies on Oct. 29 of last year, sales had taken months to rebound in heavily damaged areas such as the Rockaways and Staten Island that were located in flood zones.

In Manhattan's financial district, sales were up 44% compared with pre-Sandy numbers, and median prices rose 15%, according to the portrait of the real estate impact of Sandy, by Streeteasy.com. Sales increased in hard-hit neighborhoods such as Howard Beach, Queenswhere sales jumped 37%, though median prices were off by 10%. Sales rebounded even in hard-hit Staten Island neighborhoods in the third quarter.

"The negative impacts were particularly acute in coastal neighborhoods in the outer boroughs," noted the report, "A Tale of Two Recoveries." "These neighborhoods have consistently underperformed."

At 250 West St., a new conversion of a TriBeCa warehouse into apartments near the Hudson River was flooded even before the first resident moved in. Now the building is nearly sold out. A 7,200 square foot, four-bedroom penthouse is about to go the market for $39.5 million.

Fewer unrepaired damaged homes are listed for sale around the city, brokers said, and the inventory of repaired homestypically with newly renovated basements, plus new boilers and electrical wiringis shrinking.

In the Rockaways, where most basements and many ground-floor levels were swamped, buyers are back, lured by lower prices. They are more worried about the availability of affordable federal flood insurance, than another giant storm, said Barbara Ferguson, an agent with Rockaway Properties. New, higher flood insurance rates are due out shortly. "If there was a silver-lining to the devastation, it is that families that grew up here and were priced out before Sandy, were now able to move back in," she said.

Billy Fitzgerald, a public school principal, said he was "a little apprehensive" when he bought a damaged, white house on Beach 145th Street in the Rockaways at steep discount earlier this year for more than $600,000. He quickly rebuilt the basement and first floor so his family could move in.

He said a storm like Sandy wouldn't occur for several hundred years. "This is where we grew up, this is all we know," he said "We are rolling the dice a little bit, but everyone who makes this type of investment is taking some risk. "

The StreetEasy study found that even the Manhattan market hit the pause button after Sandy smashed New York. In November, the listings in flood zones dipped 21%, and the number of contracts signed slipped 24%, compared with November, 2011.

But the numbers bounced back. Contract signings in TriBeCa and the West Village soared, rising faster than in other areas. By last April, contracts were up 28% from the same month, a year earlier.

Leonard Steinberg, a broker at Douglas Elliman, said many building owners worked since Sandy to reduce risk by moving utilities out of the basements. "It was a very potent, scary issue a year ago, now there is a much better understanding of how Sandy-like issues can affect a building," he said.

Mr. Steinberg is listing the new penthouse at 250 West St., along with Raphael De Niro, another Elliman broker. After Sandy, openings in the building's cellar were sealed to prevent storm-water penetration. Mr. Steinberg said such precautionary measures were less of an issue for the buyer of the penthouse, which has 120 feet of frontage on the Hudson River.

The StreetEasy study compiled sales volume and pricing data by evacuation zones, with neighborhoods in zones 1 and 2 at greatest risk of storm damage. It then compared the results to other parts of each borough.

The data showed that in Manhattan, sales in flood zones rose twice as fast as sales in other parts of the borough after Sandy: They rose 26.9% in the third quarter over the same quarter in 2012, whiles sales rose 12.3% in other parts of the borough.

In Brooklyn sales were 3.9% below pre-Sandy levels last quarter, while sales in the rest of the borough were up 9.7%. In Queens sales in flood zones lagged behind the rest of the borough until the spring before quickening in the third quarter, rising 38.3% compared with same quarter a year earlier.

There were even signs of improvement in low-lying areas of Staten Island, where Sandy caused widespread damage and flooding. Median prices, down by 42.8% in the second quarter of this year from pre-Sandy levels, were down only by 23.7% by the end of the third quarter. And sales in the third quarter recovered from a dramatic decline earlier in the year to nearly equal those in the quarter before Sandy.
